alpsn10{_}572{_}or was it not He who whispered to her to tell the falsehood--to tell it with a circumstance, without conscientious scruple, not once only, but to make a practice of it, so as to be a plausible, habitual, and professional liar for some six weeks or so in the year?
alpsn10{_}573{_}I imagine so.
alpsn10{_}574{_}When I was young I used to read in good books that it was God who taught the bird to make her nest, and if so He probably taught each species the other domestic arrangements best suited to it.
alpsn10{_}575{_}Or did the nest-building information come from God, and was there an evil one among the birds also who taught them at any rate to steer clear of priggishness?
alpsn10{_}576{_}Think of the spider again--an ugly creature, but I suppose God likes it.
alpsn10{_}577{_}What a mean and odious lie is that web which naturalists extol as such a marvel of ingenuity!
alpsn10{_}578{_}Once on a summer afternoon in a far country I met one of those orchids who make it their business to imitate a fly with their petals.
alpsn10{_}579{_}This lie they dispose so cunningly that real flies, thinking the honey is being already plundered, pass them without molesting them.
alpsn10{_}580{_}Watching intently and keeping very still, methought I heard this orchid speaking to the offspring which she felt within her, though I saw them not.
alpsn10{_}581{_}"My children," she exclaimed, "I must soon leave you; think upon the fly, my loved ones, for this is truth; cling to this great thought in your passage through life, for it is the one thing needful; once lose sight of it and you are lost!"
alpsn10{_}582{_}Over and over again she sang this burden in a small still voice, and so I left her.
alpsn10{_}583{_}Then straightway I came upon some butterflies whose profession it was to pretend to believe in all manner of vital truths which in their inner practice they rejected; thus, asserting themselves to be certain other and hateful butterflies which no bird will eat by reason of their abominable smell, these cunning ones conceal their own sweetness, and live long in the land and see good days.
alpsn10{_}584{_}No:  lying is so deeply rooted in nature that we may expel it with a fork, and yet it will always come back again:  it is like the poor, we must have it always with us; we must all eat a peck of moral dirt before we die.
alpsn10{_}585{_}All depends upon who it is that is lying.
alpsn10{_}586{_}One man may steal a horse when another may not look over a hedge.
alpsn10{_}587{_}The good man who tells no lies wittingly to himself and is never unkindly, may lie and lie and lie whenever he chooses to other people, and he will not be false to any man:  his lies become truths as they pass into the hearers' ear.
alpsn10{_}588{_}If a man deceives himself and is unkind, the truth is not in him, it turns to falsehood while yet in his mouth, like the quails in the Wilderness of Sinai.
alpsn10{_}589{_}How this is so or why, I know not, but that the Lord hath mercy on whom He will have mercy and whom He willeth He hardeneth.
alpsn10{_}590{_}My Italian friends are doubtless in the main right about the priests, but there are many exceptions, as they themselves gladly admit.
alpsn10{_}591{_}For my own part I have found the curato in the small subalpine villages of North Italy to be more often than not a kindly excellent man to whom I am attracted by sympathies deeper than any mere superficial differences of opinion can counteract.
alpsn10{_}592{_}With monks, however, as a general rule I am less able to get on: nevertheless, I have received much courtesy at the hands of some.
alpsn10{_}593{_}My young friend the novice was delightful--only it was so sad to think of the future that is before him.
alpsn10{_}594{_}He wanted to know all about England, and when I told him it was an island, clasped his hands and said, "Oh che Provvidenza!"
alpsn10{_}595{_}He told me how the other young men of his own age plagued him as he trudged his rounds high up among the most distant hamlets begging alms for the poor.
alpsn10{_}596{_}"Be a good fellow," they would say to him, "drop all this nonsense and come back to us, and we will never plague you again."
alpsn10{_}597{_}Then he would turn upon them and put their words from him.
alpsn10{_}598{_}Of course my sympathies were with the other young men rather than with him, but it was impossible not to be sorry for the manner in which he had been humbugged from the day of his birth, till he was now incapable of seeing things from any other standpoint than that of authority.
alpsn10{_}599{_}What he said to me about knowing that Handel was a Catholic by his music, put me in mind of what another good Catholic once said to me about a picture.
alpsn10{_}600{_}He was a Frenchman and very nice, but a devot, and anxious to convert me.
alpsn10{_}601{_}He paid a few days' visit to London, so I showed him the National Gallery.
alpsn10{_}602{_}While there I pointed out to him Sebastian del Piombo's picture of the raising of Lazarus as one of the supposed masterpieces of our collection.
alpsn10{_}603{_}He had the proper orthodox fit of admiration over it, and then we went through the other rooms.
alpsn10{_}604{_}After a while we found ourselves before West's picture of "Christ healing the sick."
alpsn10{_}605{_}My French friend did not, I suppose, examine it very carefully, at any rate he believed he was again before the raising of Lazarus by Sebastian del Piombo; he paused before it and had his fit of admiration over again:  then turning to me he said, "Ah!
alpsn10{_}606{_}you would understand this picture better if you were a Catholic."
alpsn10{_}607{_}I did not tell him of the mistake he had made, but I thought even a Protestant after a certain amount of experience would learn to see some difference between Benjamin West and Sebastian del Piombo.
alpsn10{_}608{_}From Calonico I went down into the main road and walked to Giornico, taking the right bank of the river from the bridge at the top of the Biaschina.
alpsn10{_}609{_}Not a sod of the railway was as yet turned.
alpsn10{_}610{_}At Giornico I visited the grand old church of S. Nicolao, which, though a later foundation than the church at Mairengo, retains its original condition, and appears, therefore, to be much the older of the two.
alpsn10{_}611{_}The stones are very massive, and the courses are here and there irregular as in Cyclopean walls; the end wall is not bonded into the side walls but simply built between them; the main door is very fine, and there is a side door also very good.
alpsn10{_}612{_}There are two altars one above the other, as in the churches of S. Abbondio and S. Cristoforo at Como, but I could not make the lower altar intelligible in my sketch, and indeed could hardly see it, so was obliged to leave it out.
alpsn10{_}613{_}The remains of some very early frescoes can be seen, but I did not think them remarkable.
alpsn10{_}614{_}Altogether, however, the church is one which no one should miss seeing who takes an interest in early architecture.
alpsn10{_}615{_}While painting the study from which the following sketch is taken, I was struck with the wonderfully vivid green which the whitewashed vault of the chancel and the arch dividing the chancel from the body of the church took by way of reflection from the grass and trees outside.
alpsn10{_}616{_}It is not easy at first to see how the green manages to find its way inside the church, but the grass seems to get in everywhere.
alpsn10{_}617{_}I had already often seen green reflected from brilliant pasturage on to the shadow under the eaves of whitewashed houses, but I never saw it suffuse a whole interior as it does on a fine summer's day at Giornico.
alpsn10{_}618{_}I do not remember to have seen this effect in England.
alpsn10{_}619{_}Looking up again against the mountain through the open door of the church when the sun was in a certain position, I could see an infinity of insect life swarming throughout the air.
alpsn10{_}620{_}No one could have suspected its existence, till the sun's rays fell on the wings of these small creatures at a proper angle; on this they became revealed against the darkness of the mountain behind them.
alpsn10{_}621{_}The swallows that were flying among them cannot have to hunt them, they need only fly with their mouths wide open and they must run against as many as will be good for them.
alpsn10{_}622{_}I saw this incredibly multitudinous swarm extending to a great height, and am satisfied that it was no more than what is always present during the summer months, though it is only visible in certain lights.
alpsn10{_}623{_}To these minute creatures the space between the mountains on the two sides of the Ticino valley must be as great as that between England and America to a codfish.
alpsn10{_}624{_}Many, doubtless, live in the mid-air, and never touch the bottom or sides of the valley, except at birth and death, if then.
alpsn10{_}625{_}No doubt some atmospheric effects of haze on a summer's afternoon are due to nothing but these insects.
alpsn10{_}626{_}What, again, do the smaller of them live upon?
alpsn10{_}627{_}On germs, which to them are comfortable mouthfuls, though to us invisible even with a microscope?
alpsn10{_}628{_}I find nothing more in my notes about Giornico except that the people are very handsome, and, as I thought, of a Roman type.
alpsn10{_}629{_}The place was a Roman military station, but it does not follow that the soldiers were Romans; nevertheless, there is a strain of bullet- headed blood in the place.
alpsn10{_}630{_}Also I remember being told in 1869 that two bears had been killed in the mountains above Giornico the preceding year.
alpsn10{_}631{_}At Giornico the vine begins to grow lustily, and wine is made.
alpsn10{_}632{_}The vines are trellised, and looking down upon them one would think one could walk upon them as upon a solid surface, so closely and luxuriantly do they grow.
alpsn10{_}633{_}From Giornico I began to turn my steps homeward in company with an engineer who was also about to walk back to Faido, but we resolved to take Chironico on our way, and kept therefore to the right bank of the river.
alpsn10{_}634{_}After about three or four kilometres from Giornico we reached Chironico, which is well placed upon a filled-up lake and envied as a paese ricco, but is not so captivating as some others.
alpsn10{_}635{_}Hence we ascended till at last we reached Gribbio (3960 ft.), a collection of chalets inhabited only for a short time in the year, but a nice place in summer, rich in gentians and sulphur- coloured anemones.
alpsn10{_}636{_}From Gribbio there is a path to Dalpe, offering no difficulty whatever and perfect in its way.
alpsn10{_}637{_}On this occasion, however, we went straight back to Faido by a rather shorter way than the ordinary path, and this certainly was a little difficult, or as my companion called it, "un tantino difficoltoso," in one or two places; I at least did not quite like them.
alpsn10{_}638{_}Another day I went to Lavorgo, below Calonico, and thence up to Anzonico.
alpsn10{_}639{_}The church and churchyard at Anzonico are very good; from Anzonico there is a path to Cavagnago--which is also full of good bits for sketching--and Sobrio.
alpsn10{_}640{_}The highest villages in the immediate neighbourhood of Faido are Campello and Molare; they can be seen from the market-place of the town, and are well worth the trouble of a climb.
alpsn10{_}641{_}CHAPTER VI--Piora    An excursion which may be very well made from Faido is to the Val Piora, which I have already more than once mentioned.
alpsn10{_}642{_}There is a large hotel here which has been opened some years, but has not hitherto proved the success which it was hoped it would be.
alpsn10{_}643{_}I have stayed there two or three times and found it very comfortable; doubtless, now that Signor Lombardi of the Hotel Prosa has taken it, it will become a more popular place of resort.
alpsn10{_}644{_}I took a trap from Faido to Ambri, and thence walked over to Quinto; here the path begins to ascend, and after an hour Ronco is reached.
alpsn10{_}645{_}There is a house at Ronco where refreshments and excellent Faido beer can be had.
alpsn10{_}646{_}The old lady who keeps the house would make a perfect Fate; I saw her sitting at her window spinning, and looking down over the Ticino valley as though it were the world and she were spinning its destiny.
alpsn10{_}647{_}She had a somewhat stern expression, thin lips, iron-grey eyes, and an aquiline nose; her scanty locks straggled from under the handkerchief which she wore round her head.
alpsn10{_}648{_}Her employment and the wistful far-away look she cast upon the expanse below made a very fine ensemble.
alpsn10{_}649{_}"She would have afforded," as Sir Walter Scott says, "a study for a Rembrandt, had that celebrated painter existed at the period," {9} but she must have been a smart-looking handsome girl once.
alpsn10{_}650{_}She brightened up in conversation.
alpsn10{_}651{_}I talked about Piora, which I already knew, and the Lago Tom, the highest of the three lakes.
alpsn10{_}652{_}She said she knew the Lago Tom.
alpsn10{_}653{_}I said laughingly, "Oh, I have no doubt you do.
alpsn10{_}654{_}We've had many a good day at the Lago Tom, I know."
alpsn10{_}655{_}She looked down at once.
alpsn10{_}656{_}In spite of her nearly eighty years she was active as a woman of forty, and altogether she was a very grand old lady.
alpsn10{_}657{_}Her house is scrupulously clean.
alpsn10{_}658{_}While I watched her spinning, I thought of what must so often occur to summer visitors.
alpsn10{_}659{_}I mean what sort of a look-out the old woman must have in winter, when the wind roars and whistles, and the snow drives down the valley with a fury of which we in England can have little conception.
alpsn10{_}660{_}What a place to see a snowstorm from!
alpsn10{_}661{_}and what a place from which to survey the landscape next morning after the storm is over and the air is calm and brilliant.
alpsn10{_}662{_}There are such mornings:  I saw one once, but I was at the bottom of the valley and not high up, as at Ronco.
alpsn10{_}663{_}Ronco would take a little sun even in midwinter, but at the bottom of the valley there is no sun for weeks and weeks together; all is in deep shadow below, though the upper hillsides may be seen to have the sun upon them.
alpsn10{_}664{_}I walked once on a frosty winter's morning from Airolo to Giornico, and can call to mind nothing in its way more beautiful:  everything was locked in frost--there was not a waterwheel but was sheeted and coated with ice:  the road was hard as granite--all was quiet and seen as through a dark but incredibly transparent medium.
alpsn10{_}665{_}Near Piotta I met the whole village dragging a large tree; there were many men and women dragging at it, but they had to pull hard and they were silent; as I passed them I thought what comely, well-begotten people they were.
alpsn10{_}666{_}Then, looking up, there was a sky, cloudless and of the deepest blue, against which the snow-clad mountains stood out splendidly.
alpsn10{_}667{_}No one will regret a walk in these valleys during the depth of winter.
alpsn10{_}668{_}But I should have liked to have looked down from the sun into the sunlessness, as the old Fate woman at Ronco can do when she sits in winter at her window; or again, I should like to see how things would look from this same window on a leaden morning in midwinter after snow has fallen heavily and the sky is murky and much darker than the earth.
alpsn10{_}669{_}When the storm is at its height, the snow must search and search and search even through the double windows with which the houses are protected.
alpsn10{_}670{_}It must rest upon the frames of the pictures of saints, and of the sister's "grab," and of the last hours of Count Ugolino, which adorn the walls of the parlour.
alpsn10{_}671{_}No wonder there is a S. Maria della Neve--a "St. Mary of the Snow"; but I do wonder that she has not been painted.
alpsn10{_}672{_}From Ronco the path keeps level and then descends a little so as to cross the stream that comes down from Piora.
alpsn10{_}673{_}This is near the village of Altanca, the church of which looks remarkably well from here.
alpsn10{_}674{_}Then there is an hour and a half's rapid ascent, and at last all on a sudden one finds one's self on the Lago Ritom, close to the hotel.
alpsn10{_}675{_}The lake is about a mile, or a mile and a half, long, and half a mile broad.
alpsn10{_}676{_}It is 6000 feet above the sea, very deep at the lower end, and does not freeze where the stream issues from it, so that the magnificent trout in the, lake can get air and live through the winter.
alpsn10{_}677{_}In many other lakes, as for example the Lago di Tremorgio, they cannot do this, and hence perish, though the lakes have been repeatedly stocked.
alpsn10{_}678{_}The trout in the Lago Ritom are said to be the finest in the world, and certainly I know none so fine myself.
alpsn10{_}679{_}They grow to be as large as moderate-sized salmon, and have a deep red flesh, very firm and full of flavour.
alpsn10{_}680{_}I had two cutlets off one for breakfast and should have said they were salmon unless I had known otherwise.
alpsn10{_}681{_}In winter, when the lake is frozen over, the people bring their hay from the farther Lake of Cadagno in sledges across the Lake Ritom.
alpsn10{_}682{_}Here, again, winter must be worth seeing, but on a rough snowy day Piora must be an awful place.
alpsn10{_}683{_}There are a few stunted pines near the hotel, but the hillsides are for the most part bare and green.
alpsn10{_}684{_}Piora in fact is a fine breezy open upland valley of singular beauty, and with a sweet atmosphere of cow about it; it is rich in rhododendrons, and all manner of Alpine flowers, just a trifle bleak, but as bracing as the Engadine itself.
alpsn10{_}685{_}The first night I was ever in Piora there was a brilliant moon, and the unruffled surface of the lake took the reflection of the mountains.
alpsn10{_}686{_}I could see the cattle a mile off, and hear the tinkling of their bells which danced multitudinously before the ear as fireflies come and go before the eyes; for all through a fine summer's night the cattle will feed as though it were day.
alpsn10{_}687{_}A little above the lake I came upon a man in a cave before a furnace, burning lime, and he sat looking into the fire with his back to the moonlight.
alpsn10{_}688{_}He was a quiet moody man, and I am afraid I bored him, for I could get hardly anything out of him but "Oh altro"--polite but not communicative.
alpsn10{_}689{_}So after a while I left him with his face burnished as with gold from the fire, and his back silver with the moonbeams; behind him were the pastures and the reflections in the lake and the mountains; and the distant cowbells were ringing.
alpsn10{_}690{_}Then I wandered on till I came to the chapel of S. Carlo; and in a few minutes found myself on the Lago di Cadagno.
alpsn10{_}691{_}Here I heard that there were people, and the people were not so much asleep as the simple peasantry of these upland valleys are expected to be by nine o'clock in the evening.
alpsn10{_}692{_}For now was the time when they had moved up from Ronco, Altanca, and other villages in some numbers to cut the hay, and were living for a fortnight or three weeks in the chalets upon the Lago di Cadagno.
alpsn10{_}693{_}As I have said, there is a chapel, but I doubt whether it is attended during this season with the regularity with which the parish churches of Ronco, Altanca, &c., are attended during the rest of the year.
alpsn10{_}694{_}The young people, I am sure, like these annual visits to the high places, and will be hardly weaned from them.
alpsn10{_}695{_}Happily the hay will be always there, and will have to be cut by some one, and the old people will send the young ones.
alpsn10{_}696{_}As I was thinking of these things, I found myself going off into a doze, and thought the burnished man from the furnace came up and sat beside me, and laid his hand upon my shoulder.
alpsn10{_}697{_}Then I saw the green slopes that rise all round the lake were much higher than I had thought; they went up thousands of feet, and there were pine forests upon them, while two large glaciers came down in streams that ended in a precipice of ice, falling sheer into the lake.
alpsn10{_}698{_}The edges of the mountains against the sky were rugged and full of clefts, through which I saw thick clouds of dust being blown by the wind as though from the other side of the mountains.
alpsn10{_}699{_}And as I looked, I saw that this was not dust, but people coming in crowds from the other side, but so small as to be visible at first only as dust.
alpsn10{_}700{_}And the people became musicians, and the mountainous amphitheatre a huge orchestra, and the glaciers were two noble armies of women-singers in white robes, ranged tier above tier behind each other, and the pines became orchestral players, while the thick dust-like cloud of chorus-singers kept pouring in through the clefts in the precipices in inconceivable numbers.
alpsn10{_}701{_}When I turned my telescope upon them I saw they were crowded up to the extreme edge of the mountains, so that I could see underneath the soles of their boots as their legs dangled in the air.
alpsn10{_}702{_}In the midst of all, a precipice that rose from out of the glaciers shaped itself suddenly into an organ, and there was one whose face I well knew sitting at the keyboard, smiling and pluming himself like a bird as he thundered forth a giant fugue by way of overture.
alpsn10{_}703{_}I heard the great pedal notes in the bass stalk majestically up and down, like the rays of the Aurora that go about upon the face of the heavens off the coast of Labrador.
alpsn10{_}704{_}Then presently the people rose and sang the chorus "Venus laughing from the skies;" but ere the sound had well died away, I awoke, and all was changed; a light fleecy cloud had filled the whole basin, but I still thought I heard a sound of music, and a scampering-off of great crowds from the part where the precipices should be.
alpsn10{_}705{_}The music went thus:- {10}  [At this point in the book a music score is given]  By and by the cantering, galloping movement became a trotting one, thus:-  [At this point in the book a music score is given]  After that I heard no more but a little singing from the chalets, and turned homewards.
alpsn10{_}706{_}When I got to the chapel of S. Carlo, I was in the moonlight again, and when near the hotel, I passed the man at the mouth of the furnace with the moon still gleaming upon his back, and the fire upon his face, and he was very grave and quiet.
alpsn10{_}707{_}Next morning I went along the lake till I came to a good-sized streamlet on the north side.
alpsn10{_}708{_}If this is followed for half-an-hour or so--and the walk is a very good one--Lake Tom is reached, about 7500 feet above the sea.
alpsn10{_}709{_}The lake is not large, and there are not so many chalets as at Cadagno; still there are some.
alpsn10{_}710{_}The view of the mountain tops on the other side the Ticino valley, as seen from across the lake, is very fine.
alpsn10{_}711{_}I tried to sketch, but was fairly driven back by a cloud of black gnats.
alpsn10{_}712{_}The ridges immediately at the back of the lake, and no great height above it, are the main dividing line of the watershed; so are those that rise from the Lago di Cadagno; in fact, about 600 feet above this lake is the top of a pass which goes through the Piano dei Porci, and leads down to S. Maria Maggiore, on the German side of the Lukmanier.
alpsn10{_}713{_}I do not know the short piece between the Lago di Cadagno and S. Maria, but it is sure to be good.
alpsn10{_}714{_}It is a pity there is no place at S. Maria where one can put up for a night or two.
alpsn10{_}715{_}There is a small inn there, but it did not look tempting.
alpsn10{_}716{_}Before leaving the Val Leventina, I would call attention to the beautiful old parish church at Biasca, where there is now an excellent inn, the Hotel Biasca.
alpsn10{_}717{_}This church is not so old as the one at Giornico, but it is a good though plain example of early Lombard architecture.
alpsn10{_}718{_}CHAPTER VII--S. Michele and the Monte Pirchiriano    Some time after the traveller from Paris to Turin has passed through the Mont Cenis tunnel, and shortly before he arrives at Bussoleno station, the line turns eastward, and a view is obtained of the valley of the Dora, with the hills beyond Turin, and the Superga, in the distance.
alpsn10{_}719{_}On the right-hand side of the valley and about half-way between Susa and Turin the eye is struck by an abruptly-descending mountain with a large building like a castle upon the top of it, and the nearer it is approached the more imposing does it prove to be.
alpsn10{_}720{_}Presently the mountain is seen more edgeways, and the shape changes.
alpsn10{_}721{_}In half-an-hour or so from this point, S. Ambrogio is reached, once a thriving town, where carriages used to break the journey between Turin and Susa, but left stranded since the opening of the railway.
alpsn10{_}722{_}Here we are at the very foot of the Monte Pirchiriano, for so the mountain is called, and can see the front of the building--which is none other than the famous sanctuary of S. Michele, commonly called "della Chiusa," from the wall built here by Desiderius, king of the Lombards, to protect his kingdom from Charlemagne.
alpsn10{_}723{_}The history of the sanctuary is briefly as follows:-  At the close of the tenth century, when Otho III was Emperor of Germany, a certain Hugh de Montboissier, a noble of Auvergne, commonly called "Hugh the Unsewn" (lo sdruscito), was commanded by the Pope to found a monastery in expiation of some grave offence.
alpsn10{_}724{_}He chose for his site the summit of the Monte Pirchiriano in the valley of Susa, being attracted partly by the fame of a church already built there by a recluse of Ravenna, Giovanni Vincenzo by name, and partly by the striking nature of the situation.
alpsn10{_}725{_}Hugh de Montboissier when returning from Rome to France with Isengarde his wife, would, as a matter of course, pass through the valley of Susa.
alpsn10{_}726{_}The two--perhaps when stopping to dine at S. Ambrogio--would look up and observe the church founded by Giovanni Vincenzo:  they had got to build a monastery somewhere; it would very likely, therefore, occur to them that they could not perpetuate their names better than by choosing this site, which was on a much travelled road, and on which a fine building would show to advantage.
alpsn10{_}727{_}If my view is correct, we have here an illustration of a fact which is continually observable--namely, that all things which come to much, whether they be books, buildings, pictures, music, or living beings, are suggested by others of their own kind.
alpsn10{_}728{_}It is; always the most successful, like Handel and Shakespeare, who owe most to their forerunners, in spite of the modifications with which their works descend.
alpsn10{_}729{_}Giovanni Vincenzo had built his church about the year 987.
alpsn10{_}730{_}It is maintained by some that he had been Bishop of Ravenna, but Claretta gives sufficient reason for thinking otherwise.
alpsn10{_}731{_}In the "Cronaca Clusina" it is said that he had for some years previously lived as a recluse on the Monte Caprasio, to the north of the present Monte Pirchiriano; but that one night he had a vision, in which he saw the summit of Monte Pirchiriano enveloped in heaven-descended flames, and on this founded a church there, and dedicated it to St. Michael.
alpsn10{_}732{_}This is the origin of the name Pirchiriano, which means [Greek text], or the Lord's fire.
alpsn10{_}733{_}The fame of the heavenly flames and the piety of pilgrims brought in enough money to complete the building--which, to judge from the remains of it embodied in the later work, must have been small, but still a church, and more than a mere chapel or oratory.
alpsn10{_}734{_}It was, as I have already suggested, probably imposing enough to fire the imagination of Hugh de Montboissier, and make him feel the capabilities of the situation, which a mere ordinary wayside chapel might perhaps have failed to do.
alpsn10{_}735{_}Having built his church, Giovanni Vincenzo returned to his solitude on the top of Monte Caprasio, and thenceforth went backwards and forwards from one place of abode to the other.
alpsn10{_}736{_}Avogadro is among those who make Giovanni Bishop, or rather Archbishop, of Ravenna, and gives the following account of the circumstances which led to his resigning his diocese and going to live at the top of the inhospitable Monte Caprasio.
alpsn10{_}737{_}It seems there had been a confirmation at Ravenna, during which he had accidentally forgotten to confirm the child of a certain widow.
alpsn10{_}738{_}The child, being in weakly health, died before Giovanni could repair his oversight, and this preyed upon his mind.
alpsn10{_}739{_}In answer, however, to his earnest prayers, it pleased the Almighty to give him power to raise the dead child to life again:  this he did, and having immediately performed the rite of confirmation, restored the boy to his overjoyed mother.
alpsn10{_}740{_}He now became so much revered that he began to be alarmed lest pride should obtain dominion over him; he felt, therefore, that his only course was to resign his diocese, and go and live the life of a recluse on the top of some high mountain.
alpsn10{_}741{_}It is said that he suffered agonies of doubt as to whether it was not selfish of him to take such care of his own eternal welfare, at the expense of that of his flock, whom no successor could so well guide and guard from evil; but in the end he took a reasonable view of the matter, and concluded that his first duty was to secure his own spiritual position.
alpsn10{_}742{_}Nothing short of the top of a very uncomfortable mountain could do this, so he at once resigned his bishopric and chose Monte Caprasio as on the whole the most comfortable uncomfortable mountain he could find.
alpsn10{_}743{_}The latter part of the story will seem strange to Englishmen.
alpsn10{_}744{_}We can hardly fancy the Archbishop of Canterbury or York resigning his diocese and settling down quietly on the top of Scafell or Cader Idris to secure his eternal welfare.
alpsn10{_}745{_}They would hardly do so even on the top of Primrose Hill.
alpsn10{_}746{_}But nine hundred years ago human nature was not the same as nowadays.
alpsn10{_}747{_}The valley of Susa, then little else than marsh and forest, was held by a marquis of the name of Arduin, a descendant of a French or Norman adventurer Roger, who, with a brother, also named Arduin, had come to seek his fortune in Italy at the beginning of the tenth century.
alpsn10{_}748{_}Roger had a son, Arduin Glabrio, who recovered the valley of Susa from the Saracens, and established himself at Susa, at the junction of the roads that come down from Mont Cenis and the Mont Genevre.
alpsn10{_}749{_}He built a castle here which commanded the valley, and was his base of operations as Lord of the Marches and Warden of the Alps.
alpsn10{_}750{_}Hugh de Montboissier applied to Arduin for leave to build upon the Monte Pirchiriano.
alpsn10{_}751{_}Arduin was then holding his court at Avigliana, a small town near S. Ambrogio, even now singularly little altered, and full of mediaeval remains; he not only gave his consent, but volunteered to sell a site to the monastery, so as to ensure it against future disturbance.
alpsn10{_}752{_}The first church of Giovanni Vincenzo had been built upon whatever little space could be found upon the top of the mountain, without, so far as I can gather, enlarging the ground artificially.
alpsn10{_}753{_}The present church--the one, that is to say, built by Hugh de Montboissier about A.D. 1000--rests almost entirely upon stone piers and masonry.
alpsn10{_}754{_}The rock has been masked by a lofty granite wall of several feet in thickness, which presents something of a keep-like appearance.
alpsn10{_}755{_}The spectator naturally imagines that there are rooms, &c., behind this wall, whereas in point of fact there is nothing but the staircase leading up to the floor of the church.
alpsn10{_}756{_}Arches spring from this masking wall, and are continued thence until the rock is reached; it is on the level surface thus obtained that the church rests.
alpsn10{_}757{_}The true floor, therefore, does not begin till near what appears from the outside to be the top of the building.
alpsn10{_}758{_}There is some uncertainty as to the exact date of the foundation of the monastery, but Claretta {11} inclines decidedly to the date 999, as against 966, the one assigned by Mabillon and Torraneo.
alpsn10{_}759{_}Claretta relies on the discovery, by Provana, of a document in the royal archives which seems to place the matter beyond dispute.
alpsn10{_}760{_}The first abbot was undoubtedly Avverto or Arveo, who established the rules of the Benedictine Order in his monastery.
alpsn10{_}761{_}"In the seven hours of daily work prescribed by the Benedictine rule," writes Cesare Balbo, "innumerable were the fields they ploughed, and the houses they built in deserts, while in more frequented places men were laying cultivated ground waste, and destroying buildings: innumerable, again, were the works of the holy fathers and of ancient authors which were copied and preserved."
alpsn10{_}762{_}{12}  From this time forward the monastery received gifts in land and privileges, and became in a few years the most important religious establishment in that part of Italy.
alpsn10{_}763{_}There have been several fires--one, among others, in the year 1340, which destroyed a great part of the monastery, and some of the deeds under which it held valuable grants; but though the part inhabited by the monks may have been rebuilt or added to, the church is certainly untouched.
alpsn10{_}764{_}CHAPTER VIII--S. Michele (continued)    I had often seen this wonderful pile of buildings, and had marvelled at it, as all must do who pass from Susa to Turin, but I never went actually up to it till last summer, in company with my friend and collaborateur, Mr. H. F. Jones.
alpsn10{_}765{_}We reached S. Ambrogio station one sultry evening in July, and, before many minutes were over, were on the path that leads to San Pietro, a little more than an hour's walk above S. Ambrogio.
alpsn10{_}766{_}In spite of what I have said about Kent, Surrey, and Sussex, we found ourselves thinking how thin and wanting, as it were, in adipose cushion is every other country in comparison with Italy; but the charm is enhanced in these days by the feeling that it can be reached so easily.
alpsn10{_}767{_}Wednesday morning, Fleet Street; Thursday evening, a path upon the quiet mountain side, under the overspreading chestnuts, with Lombardy at one's feet.
alpsn10{_}768{_}Some twenty minutes after we had begun to climb, the sanctuary became lost to sight, large drops of thunder-rain began to fall, and by the time we reached San Pietro it was pouring heavily, and had become quite dark.
alpsn10{_}769{_}An hour or so later the sky had cleared, and there was a splendid moon:  opening the windows, we found ourselves looking over the tops of trees on to some lovely upland pastures, on a winding path through which we could almost fancy we saw a youth led by an angel, and there was a dog with him, and he held a fish in his hand.
alpsn10{_}770{_}Far below were lights from villages in the valley of the Dora.
alpsn10{_}771{_}Above us rose the mountains, bathed in shadow, or glittering in the moonbeams, and there came from them the pleasant murmuring of streamlets that had been swollen by the storm.
alpsn10{_}772{_}Next morning the sky was cloudless and the air invigorating.
alpsn10{_}773{_}S. Ambrogio, at the foot of the mountain, must be some 800 feet above the sea, and San Pietro about 1500 feet above S. Ambrogio.
alpsn10{_}774{_}The sanctuary at the top of the mountain is 2800 feet above the sea- level, or about 500 feet above San Pietro.
alpsn10{_}775{_}A situation more delightful than that of San Pietro it is impossible to conceive.
alpsn10{_}776{_}It contains some 200 inhabitants, and lies on a ledge of level land, which is, of course, covered with the most beautifully green grass, and in spring carpeted with wild-flowers; great broad-leaved chestnuts rise from out the meadows, and beneath their shade are strewn masses of sober mulberry-coloured rock; but above all these rises the great feature of the place, from which, when it is in sight, the eyes can hardly be diverted,--I mean the sanctuary of S. Michele itself.
alpsn10{_}777{_}A sketch gives but little idea of the place.
alpsn10{_}778{_}In nature it appears as one of those fascinating things like the smoke from Vesuvius, or the town on the Sacro Monte at Varese, which take possession of one to the exclusion of all else, as long as they are in sight.
alpsn10{_}779{_}From each point of view it becomes more and more striking.
alpsn10{_}780{_}Climbing up to it from San Pietro and getting at last nearly on a level with the lower parts of the building, or again keeping to a pathway along the side of the mountain towards Avigliana, it will come as on the following page.
alpsn10{_}781{_}[At this point there is a picture in the book]  There is a very beautiful view from near the spot where the first of these sketches is taken.
alpsn10{_}782{_}We are then on the very ridge or crest of the mountain, and look down on the one hand upon the valley of the Dora going up to Susa, with the glaciers of the Mont Cenis in the background, and on the other upon the plains near Turin, with the colline bounding the horizon.
alpsn10{_}783{_}Immediately beneath is seen the glaring white straight line of the old Mont Cenis road, looking much more important than the dingy narrow little strip of railroad that has superseded it.
alpsn10{_}784{_}The trains that pass along the line look no bigger than caterpillars, but even at this distance they make a great roar.
alpsn10{_}785{_}If the path from which the second view is taken is followed for a quarter of an hour or so, another no less beautiful point is reached from which one can look down upon the two small lakes of Avigliana.
alpsn10{_}786{_}These lakes supply Turin with water, and, I may add, with the best water that I know of as supplied to any town.
alpsn10{_}787{_}We will now return to the place from which the first of the sketches on p. 95 was taken, and proceed to the sanctuary itself.
alpsn10{_}788{_}Passing the small but very massive circular ruin shown on the right hand of the sketch, about which nothing whatever is known either as regards its date or object, we ascend by a gentle incline to the outer gate of the sanctuary.
alpsn10{_}789{_}The battered plates of iron that cover the wooden doors are marked with many a bullet.
alpsn10{_}790{_}Then we keep under cover for a short space, after which we find ourselves at the foot of a long flight of steps.
alpsn10{_}791{_}Close by there is a little terrace with a wall round it, where one can stand and enjoy a view over the valley of the Dora to Turin.
alpsn10{_}792{_}Having ascended the steps, we are at the main entrance to the building--a massive Lombard doorway, evidently the original one.
alpsn10{_}793{_}In the space above the door there have been two frescoes, an earlier and a later one, one painted over the other, but nothing now remains save the signature of the second painter, signed in Gothic characters.
alpsn10{_}794{_}On entering, more steps must be at once climbed, and then the staircase turns at right angles and tends towards the rock.
alpsn10{_}795{_}At the head of the flight shown p. 98, the natural rock appears.
alpsn10{_}796{_}The arch above it forms a recess filled with desiccated corpses.
alpsn10{_}797{_}The great pier to the left, and, indeed, all the masonry that can be seen, has no other object than to obtain space for, and to support, the floor of the church itself.
alpsn10{_}798{_}My drawing was taken from about the level of the top of the archway through which the building is entered.
alpsn10{_}799{_}There comes in at this point a third small staircase from behind; ascending this, one finds one's self in the window above the door, from the balcony of which there is a marvellous panorama.
alpsn10{_}800{_}I took advantage of the window to measure the thickness of the walls, and found them a little over seven feet thick and built of massive granite blocks.
alpsn10{_}801{_}The stones on the inside are so sharp and clean cut that they look as if they were not more than fifty years old.
alpsn10{_}802{_}On the outside, the granite, hard as it is, is much weathered, which, indeed, considering the exposed situation, is hardly to be wondered at.
alpsn10{_}803{_}Here again how the wind must howl and whistle, and how the snow must beat in winter!
alpsn10{_}804{_}No one who has not seen snow falling during a time when the thermometer is about at zero can know how searching a thing it is.
alpsn10{_}805{_}How softly would it not lie upon the skulls and shoulders of the skeletons.
alpsn10{_}806{_}Fancy a dull dark January afternoon's twilight upon this staircase, after a heavy snow, when the soft fleece clings to the walls, having drifted in through many an opening.
alpsn10{_}807{_}Or fancy a brilliant winter's moonlight, with the moon falling upon the skeletons after snow.
alpsn10{_}808{_}And then let there be a burst of music from an organ in the church above (I am sorry to say they have only a harmonium; I wish some one would give them a fine organ).
alpsn10{_}809{_}I should like the following for example:- {13}  [At this point in the book a music score is given]  How this would sound upon these stairs, if they would leave the church-door open.
alpsn10{_}810{_}It is said in Murray's handbook that formerly the corpses which are now under the arch, used to be placed in a sitting position upon the stairs, and the peasants would crown them with flowers.
alpsn10{_}811{_}Fancy twilight or moonlight on these stairs, with the corpses sitting among the withered flowers and snow, and the pealing of a great organ.
alpsn10{_}812{_}After ascending the steps that lead towards the skeletons, we turn again sharp round to the left, and come upon another noble flight-- broad and lofty, and cut in great measure from the living rock.
alpsn10{_}813{_}At the top of this flight there are two sets of Lombard portals, both of them very fine, but in such darkness and so placed that it was impossible to get a drawing of them in detail.
alpsn10{_}814{_}After passing through them, the staircase turns again, and, as far as I can remember, some twenty or thirty steps bring one up to the level of the top of the arch which forms the recess where the corpses are.
alpsn10{_}815{_}Here there is another beautiful Lombard doorway, with a small arcade on either side which I thought English, rather than Italian, in character.
alpsn10{_}816{_}An impression was produced upon both of us that this doorway and the arcade on either side were by a different architect from the two lower archways, and from the inside of the church; or at any rate, that the details of the enrichment were cut by a different mason, or gang of masons.
alpsn10{_}817{_}I think, however, the whole doorway is in a later style, and must have been put in after some fire had destroyed the earlier one.
alpsn10{_}818{_}Opening the door, which by day is always unlocked, we found ourselves in the church itself.
alpsn10{_}819{_}As I have said, it is of pure Lombard architecture, and very good of its kind; I do not think it has been touched since the beginning of the eleventh century, except that it has been re-roofed and the pitch of the roof altered.
alpsn10{_}820{_}At the base of the most westerly of the three piers that divide the nave from the aisles, there crops out a small piece of the living rock; this is at the end farthest from the choir.
alpsn10{_}821{_}It is not likely that Giovanni Vincenzo's church reached east of this point, for from this point onwards towards the choir the floor is artificially supported, and the supporting structure is due entirely to Hugo de Montboissier.
alpsn10{_}822{_}The part of the original church which still remains is perhaps the wall, which forms the western limit of the present church.
alpsn10{_}823{_}This wall is not external.
alpsn10{_}824{_}It forms the eastern wall of a large chamber with frescoes.
alpsn10{_}825{_}I am not sure that this chamber does not occupy the whole space of the original church.
alpsn10{_}826{_}There are a few nice votive pictures in the church, and one or two very early frescoes, which are not without interest; but the main charm of the place is in the architecture, and the sense at once of age and strength which it produces.
alpsn10{_}827{_}The stock things to see are the vaults in which many of the members of the royal house of Savoy, legitimate and illegitimate, lie buried; they need not, however, be seen.
alpsn10{_}828{_}I have said that the whole building is of much about the same date, and, unless perhaps in the residential parts, about which I can say little, has not been altered.
alpsn10{_}829{_}This is not the view taken by the author of Murray's Handbook for North Italy, who says that "injudicious repairs have marred the effect of the building;" but this writer has fallen into several errors.
alpsn10{_}830{_}He talks, for example, of the "open Lombard gallery of small circular arches" as being "one of the oldest and most curious features of the building," whereas it is obviously no older than the rest of the church, nor than the keep-like construction upon which it rests.
alpsn10{_}831{_}Again, he is clearly in error when he says that the "extremely beautiful circular arch by which we pass from the staircase to the corridor leading to the church, is a vestige of the original building."
alpsn10{_}832{_}The double round arched portals through which we pass from the main staircase to the corridor are of exactly the same date as the staircase itself, and as the rest of the church.
alpsn10{_}833{_}They certainly formed no part of Giovanni Vincenzo's edifice; for, besides being far too rich, they are not on a level with what remains of that building, but several feet below it.
alpsn10{_}834{_}It is hard to know what the writer means by "the original building;" he appears to think it extended to the present choir, which, he says, "retains traces of an earlier age."
alpsn10{_}835{_}The choir retains no such traces.
alpsn10{_}836{_}The only remains of the original church are at the back of the west end, invisible from the inside of the church, and at the opposite end to the choir.
alpsn10{_}837{_}As for the church being "in a plain Gothic style," it is an extremely beautiful example of pure Lombard, of the first few years of the eleventh century.
alpsn10{_}838{_}True, the middle arch of the three which divide the nave from the aisles is pointed, whereas the two others are round, but this is evidently done to economise space, which was here unusually costly.
alpsn10{_}839{_}There was room for more than two round arches, but not room enough for three, so it was decided to dock the middle arch a little.
alpsn10{_}840{_}It is a she-arch--that is to say, it has no keystone, but is formed simply by propping two segments of a circle one against the other.
alpsn10{_}841{_}It certainly is not a Gothic arch; it is a Lombard arch, modified in an unusual manner, owing to its having been built under unusual conditions.
alpsn10{_}842{_}The visitor should on no account omit to ring the bell and ask to be shown the open Lombard gallery already referred to as running round the outside of the choir.
alpsn10{_}843{_}It is well worth walking round this, if only for the view.
alpsn10{_}844{_}The official who showed us round was very kind, and as a personal favour we were allowed to visit the fathers' private garden.
alpsn10{_}845{_}The large arm-chairs are made out of clipped box-trees.
alpsn10{_}846{_}While on our way to the garden we passed a spot where there was an alarming buzzing, and found ourselves surrounded by what appeared to be an angry swarm of bees; closer inspection showed that the host was a medley one, composed of wasps, huge hornets, hive-bees, humble- bees, flies, dragon-flies, butterflies, and all kinds of insects, flying about a single patch of ivy in full blossom, which attracted them so strongly that they neglected everything else.
alpsn10{_}847{_}I think some of them were intoxicated.
alpsn10{_}848{_}If this was so, then perhaps Bacchus is called "ivy-crowned" because ivy-blossoms intoxicate insects, but I never remember to have before observed that ivy-blossoms had any special attraction for insects.
alpsn10{_}849{_}I have forgotten to say anything about a beam of wood which may be seen standing out at right angles from the tower to the right of the main building.
alpsn10{_}850{_}This I believe to have been the gallows.
alpsn10{_}851{_}Another like it may be seen at S. Giorio, but I have not got it in my sketch of that place.
alpsn10{_}852{_}The attendant who took us round S. Michele denied that it was the gallows, but I think it must have been.
alpsn10{_}853{_}Also, the attendant showed us one place which is called Il Salto della belle Alda.
alpsn10{_}854{_}Alda was being pursued by a soldier; to preserve her honour, she leaped from a window and fell over a precipice some hundreds of feet below; by the intercession of the Virgin she was saved, but became so much elated that she determined to repeat the feat.
alpsn10{_}855{_}She jumped a second time from the window, but was dashed to pieces.
alpsn10{_}856{_}We were told this as being unworthy of actual credence, but as a legend of the place.
alpsn10{_}857{_}We said we found no great difficulty in believing the first half of the story, but could hardly believe that any one would jump from that window twice.
alpsn10{_}858{_}{14}    CHAPTER IX--The North Italian Priesthood    There is now a school in the sanctuary; we met the boys several times.
alpsn10{_}859{_}They seemed well cared for and contented.
alpsn10{_}860{_}The priests who reside in the sanctuary were courtesy itself; they took a warm interest in England, and were anxious for any information I could give them about the monastery near Loughborough--a name which they had much difficulty in pronouncing.
alpsn10{_}861{_}They were perfectly tolerant, and ready to extend to others the consideration they expected for themselves.
alpsn10{_}862{_}This should not be saying much, but as things go it is saying a good deal.
alpsn10{_}863{_}What indeed more can be wished for?
alpsn10{_}864{_}The faces of such priests as these--and I should say such priests form a full half of the North Italian priesthood--are perfectly free from that bad furtive expression which we associate with priestcraft, and which, when seen, cannot be mistaken:  their faces are those of our own best English country clergy, with perhaps a trifle less flesh about them and a trifle more of a not unkindly asceticism.
alpsn10{_}865{_}Comparing our own clergy with the best North Italian and Ticinese priests, I should say there was little to choose between them.
alpsn10{_}866{_}The latter are in a logically stronger position, and this gives them greater courage in their opinions; the former have the advantage in respect of money, and the more varied knowledge of the world which money will command.
alpsn10{_}867{_}When I say Catholics have logically the advantage over Protestants, I mean that starting from premises which both sides admit, a merely logical Protestant will find himself driven to the Church of Rome.
alpsn10{_}868{_}Most men as they grow older will, I think, feel this, and they will see in it the explanation of the comparatively narrow area over which the Reformation extended, and of the gain which Catholicism has made of late years here in England.
alpsn10{_}869{_}On the other hand, reasonable people will look with distrust upon too much reason.
alpsn10{_}870{_}The foundations of action lie deeper than reason can reach.
alpsn10{_}871{_}They rest on faith--for there is no absolutely certain incontrovertible premise which can be laid by man, any more than there is any investment for money or security in the daily affairs of life which is absolutely unimpeachable.
alpsn10{_}872{_}The funds are not absolutely sale; a volcano might break out under the Bank of England.
alpsn10{_}873{_}A railway journey is not absolutely safe; one person, at least, in several millions gets killed.
alpsn10{_}874{_}We invest our money upon faith mainly.
alpsn10{_}875{_}We choose our doctor upon faith, for how little independent judgment can we form concerning his capacity?
alpsn10{_}876{_}We choose schools for our children chiefly upon faith.
alpsn10{_}877{_}The most important things a man has are his body, his soul, and his money.
alpsn10{_}878{_}It is generally better for him to commit these interests to the care of others of whom he can know little, rather than be his own medical man, or invest his money on his own judgment; and this is nothing else than making a faith which lies deeper than reason can reach, the basis of our action in those respects which touch us most nearly.
alpsn10{_}879{_}On the other hand, as good a case could be made out for placing reason as the foundation, inasmuch as it would be easy to show that a faith, to be worth anything, must be a reasonable one--one, that is to say, which is based upon reason.
alpsn10{_}880{_}The fact is, that faith and reason are like desire and power, or demand and supply; it is impossible to say which comes first:  they come up hand in hand, and are so small when we can first descry them, that it is impossible to say which we first caught sight of.
alpsn10{_}881{_}All we can now see is that each has a tendency continually to outstrip the other by a little, but by a very little only.
alpsn10{_}882{_}Strictly they are not two things, but two aspects of one thing; for convenience sake, however, we classify them separately.
alpsn10{_}883{_}It follows, therefore--but whether it follows or no, it is certainly true--that neither faith alone nor reason alone is a sufficient guide:  a man's safety lies neither in faith nor reason, but in temper--in the power of fusing faith and reason, even when they appear most mutually destructive.
alpsn10{_}884{_}A man of temper will be certain in spite of uncertainty, and at the same time uncertain in spite of certainty; reasonable in spite of his resting mainly upon faith rather than reason, and full of faith even when appealing most strongly to reason.
alpsn10{_}885{_}If it is asked, In what should a man have faith?
alpsn10{_}886{_}To what faith should he turn when reason has led him to a conclusion which he distrusts?
alpsn10{_}887{_}the answer is, To the current feeling among those whom he most looks up to--looking upon himself with suspicion if he is either among the foremost or the laggers.
alpsn10{_}888{_}In the rough, homely common sense of the community to which we belong we have as firm ground as can be got.
alpsn10{_}889{_}This, though not absolutely infallible, is secure enough for practical purposes.
alpsn10{_}890{_}As I have said, Catholic priests have rather a fascination for me-- when they are not Englishmen.
alpsn10{_}891{_}I should say that the best North Italian priests are more openly tolerant than our English clergy generally are.
alpsn10{_}892{_}I remember picking up one who was walking along a road, and giving him a lift in my trap.
alpsn10{_}893{_}Of course we fell to talking, and it came out that I was a member of the Church of England.
alpsn10{_}894{_}"Ebbene, caro Signore," said he when we shook hands at parting; "mi rincresce che Lei non crede come me, ma in questi tempi non possiamo avere tutti i medesimi principii."
alpsn10{_}895{_}{15}  I travelled another day from Susa to S. Ambrogio with a priest, who told me he took in "The Catholic Times," and who was well up to date on English matters.
alpsn10{_}896{_}Being myself a Conservative, I found his opinions sound on all points but one--I refer to the Irish question:  he had no sympathy with the obstructionists in Parliament, but nevertheless thought the Irish were harshly treated.
alpsn10{_}897{_}I explained matters as well as I could, and found him very willing to listen to our side of the question.
alpsn10{_}898{_}The one thing, he said, which shocked him with the English, was the manner in which they went about distributing tracts upon the Continent.
alpsn10{_}899{_}I said no one could deplore the practice more profoundly than myself, but that there were stupid and conceited people in every country, who would insist upon thrusting their opinions upon people who did not want them.
alpsn10{_}900{_}He replied that the Italians travelled not a little in England, but that he was sure not one of them would dream of offering Catholic tracts to people, for example, in the streets of London.
alpsn10{_}901{_}Certainly I have never seen an Italian to be guilty of such rudeness.
alpsn10{_}902{_}It seems to me that it is not only toleration that is a duty; we ought to go beyond this now; we should conform, when we are among a sufficient number of those who would not understand our refusal to do so; any other course is to attach too much importance at once to our own opinions and to those of our opponents.
alpsn10{_}903{_}By all means let a man stand by his convictions when the occasion requires, but let him reserve his strength, unless it is imperatively called for.
alpsn10{_}904{_}Do not let him exaggerate trifles, and let him remember that everything is a trifle in comparison with the not giving offence to a large number of kindly, simple-minded people.
alpsn10{_}905{_}Evolution, as we all know, is the great doctrine of modern times; the very essence of evolution consists in the not shocking anything too violently, but enabling it to mistake a new action for an old one, without "making believe" too much.
alpsn10{_}906{_}One day when I was eating my lunch near a fountain, there came up a moody, meditative hen, crooning plaintively after her wont.
alpsn10{_}907{_}I threw her a crumb of bread while she was still a good way off, and then threw more, getting her to come a little closer and a little closer each time; at last she actually took a piece from my hand.
alpsn10{_}908{_}She did not quite like it, but she did it.
alpsn10{_}909{_}This is the evolution principle; and if we wish those who differ from us to understand us, it is the only method to proceed upon.
alpsn10{_}910{_}I have sometimes thought that some of my friends among the priests have been treating me as I treated the meditative hen.
alpsn10{_}911{_}But what of that?
alpsn10{_}912{_}They will not kill and eat me, nor take my eggs.
alpsn10{_}913{_}Whatever, therefore, promotes a more friendly feeling between us must be pure gain.
alpsn10{_}914{_}The mistake our advanced Liberals make is that of flinging much too large pieces of bread at a time, and flinging them at their hen, instead of a little way off her.
alpsn10{_}915{_}Of course the hen is fluttered and driven away.
alpsn10{_}916{_}Sometimes, too, they do not sufficiently distinguish between bread and stones.
alpsn10{_}917{_}As a general rule, the common people treat the priests respectfully, but once I heard several attacking one warmly on the score of eternal punishment.
alpsn10{_}918{_}"Sara," said one, "per cento anni, per cinque cento, per mille o forse per dieci mille anni, ma non sara eterna; perche il Dio e un uomo forte--grande, generoso, di buon cuore."
alpsn10{_}919{_}{16}  An Italian told me once that if ever I came upon a priest whom I wanted to tease, I was to ask him if he knew a place called La Torre Pellice.
alpsn10{_}920{_}I have never yet had the chance of doing this; for, though I am fairly quick at seeing whether I am likely to get on with a priest or no, I find the priest is generally fairly quick too; and I am no sooner in a diligence or railway carriage with an unsympathetic priest, than he curls himself round into a moral ball and prays horribly--bristling out with collects all over like a cross-grained spiritual hedgehog.
alpsn10{_}921{_}Partly, therefore, from having no wish to go out of my way to make myself obnoxious, and partly through the opposite party being determined that I shall not get the chance, the question about La Torre Pellice has never come off, and I do not know what a priest would say if the subject were introduced,--but I did get a talking about La Torre Pellice all the same.
alpsn10{_}922{_}I was going from Turin to Pinerolo, and found myself seated opposite a fine-looking elderly gentleman who was reading a paper headed, "Le Temoin, Echo des Vallees Vaudoises":  for the Vaudois, or Waldenses, though on the Italian side of the Alps, are French in language and perhaps in origin.
alpsn10{_}923{_}I fell to talking with this gentleman, and found he was on his way to La Torre Pellice, the headquarters of indigenous Italian evangelicism.
alpsn10{_}924{_}He told me there were about 25,000 inhabitants of these valleys, and that they were without exception Protestant, or rather that they had never accepted Catholicism, but had retained the primitive Apostolic faith in its original purity.
alpsn10{_}925{_}He hinted to me that they were descendants of some one or more of the lost ten tribes of Israel.
alpsn10{_}926{_}The English, he told me (meaning, I gather, the English of the England that affects Exeter Hall), had done great things for the inhabitants of La Torre at different times, and there were streets called the Via Williams and Via Beckwith.
alpsn10{_}927{_}They were, he said, a very growing sect, and had missionaries and establishments in all the principal cities in North Italy; in fact, so far as I could gather, they were as aggressive as malcontents generally are, and, Italians though they were, would give away tracts just as readily as we do.
alpsn10{_}928{_}I did not, therefore, go to La Torre.
alpsn10{_}929{_}Sometimes priests say things, as a matter of course, which would make any English clergyman's hair stand on end.
alpsn10{_}930{_}At one town there is a remarkable fourteenth-century bridge, commonly known as "The Devil's Bridge."
alpsn10{_}931{_}I was sketching near this when a jolly old priest with a red nose came up and began a conversation with me.
alpsn10{_}932{_}He was evidently a popular character, for every one who passed greeted him.
alpsn10{_}933{_}He told me that the devil did not really build the bridge.
alpsn10{_}934{_}I said I presumed not, for he was not in the habit of spending his time so well.
alpsn10{_}935{_}"I wish he had built it," said my friend; "for then perhaps he would build us some more."
alpsn10{_}936{_}"Or we might even get a church out of him," said I, a little slyly.
alpsn10{_}937{_}"Ha, ha, ha!
alpsn10{_}938{_}we will convert him, and make a good Christian of him in the end."
alpsn10{_}939{_}When will our Protestantism, or Rationalism, or whatever it may be, sit as lightly upon ourselves?
alpsn10{_}940{_}CHAPTER X--S. Ambrogio and Neighbourhood    Since the opening of the railway, the old inn where the diligences and private carriages used to stop has been closed; but I was made, in a homely way, extremely comfortable at the Scudo di Francia, kept by Signor Bonaudo and his wife.
alpsn10{_}941{_}I stayed here over a fortnight, during which I made several excursions.
alpsn10{_}942{_}One day I went to San Giorio, as it is always written though San Giorgio is evidently intended.
alpsn10{_}943{_}Here there is a ruined castle, beautifully placed upon a hill; this castle shows well from the railway shortly after leaving Bussoleno station, on the right hand going towards Turin.
alpsn10{_}944{_}Having been struck with it, I went by train to Bussoleno (where there is much that I was unwillingly compelled to neglect), and walked back to San Giorio.
alpsn10{_}945{_}On my way, however, I saw a patch of Cima-da-Conegliano-looking meadow-land on a hill some way above me, and on this there rose from among the chestnuts what looked like a castellated mansion.
alpsn10{_}946{_}I thought it well to make a digression to this, and when I got there, after a lovely walk, knocked at the door, having been told by peasants that there would be no difficulty about my taking a look round.
alpsn10{_}947{_}The place is called the Castel Burrello, and is tenanted by an old priest who has retired hither to end his days.
alpsn10{_}948{_}I sent in my card and business by his servant, and by-and-by he came out to me himself.
alpsn10{_}949{_}"Vous etes Anglais, monsieur?"
alpsn10{_}950{_}said he in French.
alpsn10{_}951{_}"Oui, monsieur."
alpsn10{_}952{_}"Vous etes Catholique?"
alpsn10{_}953{_}"Monsieur, je suis de la religion de mes peres."
alpsn10{_}954{_}"Pardon, monsieur, vos ancetres etaient Catholiques jusqu'au temps de Henri VIII."
alpsn10{_}955{_}"Mais il y a trois cent ans depuis le temps de Henri VIII."
alpsn10{_}956{_}"Eh bien!
alpsn10{_}957{_}chacun a ses convictions; vous ne parlez pas contre la religion?"
alpsn10{_}958{_}"Jamais, jamais, monsieur; j'ai un respect enorme pour l'Eglise Catholique."
alpsn10{_}959{_}"Monsieur, faites comme chez vous; allez ou vous voulez; vous trouverez toutes les portes ouvertes.
alpsn10{_}960{_}Amusez-vous bien."
alpsn10{_}961{_}He then explained to me that the castle had never been a properly fortified place, being intended only as a summer residence for the barons of Bussoleno, who used to resort hither during the extreme heat, if times were tolerably quiet.
alpsn10{_}962{_}After this he left me.
alpsn10{_}963{_}Taking him at his word, I walked all round, but there was only a shell remaining; the rest of the building had evidently been burnt, even the wing in which the present proprietor resides being, if I remember rightly, modernised.
alpsn10{_}964{_}The site, however, and the sloping meadows which the castle crowns, are of extreme beauty.
alpsn10{_}965{_}I now walked down to San Giorio, and found a small inn where I could get bread, butter, eggs, and good wine.
alpsn10{_}966{_}I was waited upon by a good-natured boy, the son of the landlord, who was accompanied by a hawk that sat always either upon his hand or shoulder.
alpsn10{_}967{_}As I looked at the pair I thought they were very much alike, and certainly they were very much in love with one another.
alpsn10{_}968{_}After dinner I sketched the castle.
alpsn10{_}969{_}While I was doing so, a gentleman told me that a large breach in the wall was made a few years ago, and a part of the wall found to be hollow, the bottom of the hollow part being unwittingly removed, there fell through a skeleton in a full suit of armour.
alpsn10{_}970{_}Others, whom I asked, had heard nothing of this.
alpsn10{_}971{_}Talking of hawks, I saw a good many boys with tame young hawks in the villages round about.
alpsn10{_}972{_}There was a tame hawk at the station of S. Ambrogio.
alpsn10{_}973{_}The station-master said it used to go now and again to the church-steeple to catch sparrows, but would always return in an hour or two.
alpsn10{_}974{_}Before my stay was over it got in the way of a passing train and was run over.
alpsn10{_}975{_}Young birds are much eaten in this neighbourhood.
alpsn10{_}976{_}The houses and barns, not to say the steeples of the churches, are to be seen stuck about with what look like terra-cotta water-bottles with the necks outwards.
alpsn10{_}977{_}Two or three may be seen in the illustration on p. 113 outside the window that comes out of the roof, on the left-hand side of the picture.
alpsn10{_}978{_}I have seen some outside an Italian restaurant near Lewisham.
alpsn10{_}979{_}They are artificial bird's-nests for the sparrows to build in:  as soon as the young are old enough they are taken and made into a pie.
alpsn10{_}980{_}The church-tower near the Hotel de la Poste at Lanzo is more stuck about with them than any other building that I have seen.
alpsn10{_}981{_}Swallows and hawks are about the only birds whose young are not eaten.
alpsn10{_}982{_}One afternoon I met a boy with a jay on his finger:  having imprudently made advances to this young gentleman in the hopes of getting acquainted with the bird, he said he thought I had better buy it and have it for my dinner; but I did not fancy it.
alpsn10{_}983{_}Another day I saw the padrona at the inn-door talking to a lad, who pulled open his shirt-front and showed some twenty or thirty nestlings in the simple pocket formed by his shirt on the one side and his skin upon the other.
alpsn10{_}984{_}The padrona wanted me to say I should like to eat them, in which case she would have bought them; but one cannot get all the nonsense one hears at home out of one's head in a moment, and I am afraid I preached a little.
alpsn10{_}985{_}The padrona, who is one of the most fascinating women in the world, and at sixty is still handsome, looked a little vexed and puzzled:  she admitted the truth of what I said, but pleaded that the boys found it very hard to gain a few soldi, and if people didn't kill and eat one thing, they would another.
alpsn10{_}986{_}The result of it all was that I determined for the future to leave young birds to their fate; they and the boys must settle that matter between themselves.
alpsn10{_}987{_}If the young bird was a boy, and the boy a young bird, it would have been the boy who was taken ruthlessly from his nest and eaten.
alpsn10{_}988{_}An old bird has no right to have a homestead, and a young bird has no right to exist at all, unless they can keep both homestead and existence out of the way of boys who are in want of half-pence.
alpsn10{_}989{_}It is all perfectly right, and when we go and stay among these charming people, let us do so as learners, not as teachers.
alpsn10{_}990{_}I watched the padrona getting my supper ready.
alpsn10{_}991{_}With what art do not these people manage their fire.
alpsn10{_}992{_}The New Zealand Maoris say the white man is a fool:  "He makes a large fire, and then has to sit away from it; the Maori makes a small fire, and sits over it."
alpsn10{_}993{_}The scheme of an Italian kitchen-fire is that there shall always be one stout log smouldering on the hearth, from which a few live coals may be chipped off if wanted, and put into the small square gratings which are used for stewing or roasting.
alpsn10{_}994{_}Any warming up, or shorter boiling, is done on the Maori principle of making a small fire of light dry wood, and feeding it frequently.
alpsn10{_}995{_}They economise everything.
alpsn10{_}996{_}Thus I saw the padrona wash some hen's eggs well in cold water; I did not see why she should wash them before boiling them, but presently the soup which I was to have for my supper began to boil.
alpsn10{_}997{_}Then she put the eggs into the soup and boiled them in it.
alpsn10{_}998{_}After supper I had a talk with the padrone, who told me I was working too hard.
alpsn10{_}999{_}"Totam noctem," said he in Latin, "lavoravimus et nihil incepimus."
alpsn10{_}1000{_}("We have laboured all night and taken nothing.")
alpsn10{_}1001{_}"Oh!"
alpsn10{_}1002{_}he continued, "I have eyes and ears in my head."
alpsn10{_}1003{_}And as he spoke, with his right hand he drew down his lower eyelid, and with his left pinched the pig of his ear.
alpsn10{_}1004{_}"You will be ill if you go on like this."
alpsn10{_}1005{_}Then he laid his hand along his cheek, put his head on one side, and shut his eyes, to imitate a sick man in bed.
alpsn10{_}1006{_}On this I arranged to go an excursion with him on the day following to a farm he had a few miles off, and to which he went every Friday.
alpsn10{_}1007{_}We went to Borgone station, and walked across the valley to a village called Villar Fochiardo.
alpsn10{_}1008{_}Thence we began gently to ascend, passing under some noble chestnuts.
alpsn10{_}1009{_}Signor Bonaudo said that this is one of the best chestnut-growing districts in Italy.
alpsn10{_}1010{_}A good tree, he told me, would give its forty francs a year.
alpsn10{_}1011{_}This seems as though chestnut-growing must be lucrative, for an acre should carry some five or six trees, and there is no outlay to speak of.
alpsn10{_}1012{_}Besides the chestnuts, the land gives a still further return by way of the grass that grows beneath them.
alpsn10{_}1013{_}Walnuts do not yield nearly so much per tree as chestnuts do.
alpsn10{_}1014{_}In three-quarters of an hour or so we reached Signor Bonaudo's farm, which was called the Casina di Banda.
alpsn10{_}1015{_}The buildings had once been a monastery, founded at the beginning of the seventeenth century and secularised by the first Napoleon, but had been purchased from the state a few years ago by Signor Bonaudo, in partnership with three others, after the passing of the Church Property Act.
alpsn10{_}1016{_}It is beautifully situated some hundreds of feet above the valley, and commands a lovely view of the Comba, as it is called, or Combe of Susa.
alpsn10{_}1017{_}The accompanying sketch will give an idea of the view looking towards Turin.
alpsn10{_}1018{_}The large building on the hill is, of course, S. Michele.
alpsn10{_}1019{_}The very distant dome is the Superga on the other side of Turin.
alpsn10{_}1020{_}The first thing Signor Bonaudo did when he got to his farm was to see whether the water had been duly turned on to his own portion of the estate.
alpsn10{_}1021{_}Each of the four purchasers had his separate portion, and each had a right to the water for thirty-six hours per week.
alpsn10{_}1022{_}Signor Bonaudo went round with his hind at once, and saw that the dams in the ducts were so opened or closed that his own land was being irrigated.
alpsn10{_}1023{_}Nothing can exceed the ingenuity with which the little canals are arranged so that each part of a meadow, however undulating, shall be saturated equally.
alpsn10{_}1024{_}The people are very jealous of their water rights, and indeed not unnaturally, for the yield of grass depends in very great measure upon the amount of irrigation which the land can get.
alpsn10{_}1025{_}The matter of the water having been seen to, we went to the monastery, or, as it now is, the homestead.
alpsn10{_}1026{_}As we entered the farmyard we found two cows fighting, and a great strapping wench belabouring them in order to separate them.
alpsn10{_}1027{_}"Let them alone," said the padrone; "let them fight it out here on the level ground."
alpsn10{_}1028{_}Then he explained to me that he wished them to find out which was mistress, and fall each of them into her proper place, for if they fought on the rough hillsides they might easily break each other's necks.
alpsn10{_}1029{_}We walked all over the monastery.
alpsn10{_}1030{_}The day was steamy with frequent showers, and thunderstorms in the air.
alpsn10{_}1031{_}The rooms were dark and mouldy, and smelt rather of rancid cheese, but it was not a bad sort of rambling old place, and if thoroughly done up would make a delightful inn.
alpsn10{_}1032{_}There is a report that there is hidden treasure here.
alpsn10{_}1033{_}I do not know a single old castle or monastery in North Italy about which no such report is current, but in the present case there seems more than usual ground (so the hind told me) for believing the story to be well founded, for the monks did certainly smelt the quartz in the neighbourhood, and as no gold was ever known to leave the monastery, it is most likely that all the enormous quantity which they must have made in the course of some two centuries is still upon the premises, if one could only lay one's hands upon it.
alpsn10{_}1034{_}So reasonable did this seem, that about two years ago it was resolved to call in a somnambulist or clairvoyant from Turin, who, when he arrived at the spot, became seized with convulsions, betokening of course that there was treasure not far off:  these convulsions increased till he reached the choir of the chapel, and here he swooned--falling down as if dead, and being resuscitated with apparent difficulty.
alpsn10{_}1035{_}He afterwards declared that it was in this chapel that the treasure was hidden.
alpsn10{_}1036{_}In spite of all this, however, the chapel has not been turned upside down and ransacked, perhaps from fear of offending the saint to whom it is dedicated.
alpsn10{_}1037{_}In the chapel there are a few votive pictures, but not very striking ones.
alpsn10{_}1038{_}I hurriedly sketched one, but have failed to do it justice.
alpsn10{_}1039{_}The hind saw me copying the little girl in bed, and I had an impression as though he did not quite understand my motive.
alpsn10{_}1040{_}I told him I had a dear little girl of my own at home, who had been alarmingly ill in the spring, and that this picture reminded me of her.
alpsn10{_}1041{_}This made everything quite comfortable.
alpsn10{_}1042{_}We had brought up our dinner from S. Ambrogio, and ate it in what had been the refectory of the monastery.
alpsn10{_}1043{_}The windows were broken, and the swallows, who had built upon the ceiling inside the room, kept flying close to us all the time we were eating.
alpsn10{_}1044{_}Great mallows and hollyhocks peered in at the window, and beyond them there was a pretty Devonshire-looking orchard.
alpsn10{_}1045{_}The noontide sun streamed in at intervals between the showers.
alpsn10{_}1046{_}After dinner we went "al cresto della collina"--to the crest of the hill--to use Signor Bonaudo's words, and looked down upon S. Giorio, and the other villages of the Combe of Susa.
alpsn10{_}1047{_}Nothing could be more delightful.
alpsn10{_}1048{_}Then, getting under the chestnuts, I made the sketch which I have already given.
alpsn10{_}1049{_}While making it I was accosted by an underjawed man (there is an unusually large percentage of underjawed people in the neighbourhood of S. Ambrogio), who asked whether my taking this sketch must not be considered as a sign that war was imminent.
alpsn10{_}1050{_}The people in this valley have bitter and comparatively recent experience of war, and are alarmed at anything which they fancy may indicate its recurrence.
alpsn10{_}1051{_}Talking further with him, he said, "Here we have no signori; we need not take off our hats to any one except the priest.
alpsn10{_}1052{_}We grow all we eat, we spin and weave all we wear; if all the world except our own valley were blotted out, it would make no difference, so long as we remain as we are and unmolested."
alpsn10{_}1053{_}He was a wild, weird, St. John the Baptist looking person, with shaggy hair, and an Andrea Mantegnesque feeling about him.
alpsn10{_}1054{_}I gave him a pipe of English tobacco, which he seemed to relish, and so we parted.
alpsn10{_}1055{_}I stayed a week or so at another place not a hundred miles from Susa, but I will not name it, for fear of causing offence.
alpsn10{_}1056{_}It was situated high, above the valley of the Dora, among the pastures, and just about the upper limit of the chestnuts.
alpsn10{_}1057{_}It offers a summer retreat, of which the people in Turin avail themselves in considerable numbers.
alpsn10{_}1058{_}The inn was a more sophisticated one than Signor Bonaudo's house at S. Ambrogio, and there were several Turin people staying there as well as myself, but there were no English.
alpsn10{_}1059{_}During the whole time I was in that neighbourhood I saw not a single English, French, or German tourist.
alpsn10{_}1060{_}The ways of the inn, therefore, were exclusively Italian, and I had a better opportunity of seeing the Italians as they are among themselves than I ever had before.
