alpsn10{_}1061{_}Nothing struck me more than the easy terms on which every one, including the waiter, appeared to be with every one else.
alpsn10{_}1062{_}This, which in England would be impossible, is here not only possible but a matter of course, because the general standard of good breeding is distinctly higher than it is among ourselves.
alpsn10{_}1063{_}I do not mean to say that there are no rude or unmannerly Italians, but that there are fewer in proportion than there are in any other nation with which I have acquaintance.
alpsn10{_}1064{_}This is not to be wondered at, for the Italians have had a civilisation for now some three or four thousand years, whereas all other nations are, comparatively speaking, new countries, with a something even yet of colonial roughness pervading them.
alpsn10{_}1065{_}As the colonies to England, so is England to Italy in respect of the average standard of courtesy and good manners.
alpsn10{_}1066{_}In a new country everything has a tendency to go wild again, man included; and the longer civilisation has existed in any country the more trustworthy and agreeable will its inhabitants be.
alpsn10{_}1067{_}This preface is necessary, as explaining how it is possible that things can be done in Italy without offence which would be intolerable elsewhere; but I confess to feeling rather hopeless of being able to describe what I actually saw without giving a wrong impression concerning it.
alpsn10{_}1068{_}Among the visitors was the head confidential clerk of a well-known Milanese house, with his wife and sister.
alpsn10{_}1069{_}The sister was an invalid, and so also was the husband, but the wife was a very pretty woman and a very merry one.
alpsn10{_}1070{_}The waiter was a good-looking young fellow of about five-and-twenty, and between him and Signora Bonvicino--for we will say this was the clerk's name--there sprang up a violent flirtation, all open and above board.
alpsn10{_}1071{_}The waiter was evidently very fond of her, but said the most atrociously impudent things to her from time to time.
alpsn10{_}1072{_}Dining under the veranda at the next table I heard the Signora complain that the cutlets were burnt.
alpsn10{_}1073{_}So they were--very badly burnt.
alpsn10{_}1074{_}The waiter looked at them for a moment--threw her a contemptuous glance, clearly intended to provoke war--"Chi non ha appetito {17} .
alpsn10{_}1075{_}.
alpsn10{_}1076{_}. "
alpsn10{_}1077{_}he exclaimed, and was moving off with a shrug of the shoulders.
alpsn10{_}1078{_}The Signora recognising a challenge, rose instantly from the table, and catching him by the nape of his neck, kicked him deftly downstairs into the kitchen, both laughing heartily, and the husband and sister joining.
alpsn10{_}1079{_}I never saw anything more neatly done.
alpsn10{_}1080{_}Of course, in a few minutes some fresh and quite unexceptionable cutlets made their appearance.
alpsn10{_}1081{_}Another morning, when I came down to breakfast, I found an altercation going on between the same pair as to whether the lady's nose was too large or not.
alpsn10{_}1082{_}It was not at all too large.
alpsn10{_}1083{_}It was a very pretty little nose.
alpsn10{_}1084{_}The waiter was maintaining that it was too large, and the lady that it was not.
alpsn10{_}1085{_}One evening Signor Bonvicino told me that his employer had a very large connection in England, and that though he had never been in London, he knew all about it almost as well as if he had.
alpsn10{_}1086{_}The great centre of business, he said, was in Red Lion Square.
alpsn10{_}1087{_}It was here his employer's agent resided, and this was a more important part than even the city proper.
alpsn10{_}1088{_}I threw a drop or two of cold water on this, but without avail.
alpsn10{_}1089{_}Presently I asked what the waiter's name was, not having been able to catch it.
alpsn10{_}1090{_}I asked this of the Signora, and saw a little look on her face as though she were not quite prepared to reply.
alpsn10{_}1091{_}Not understanding this, I repeated my question.
alpsn10{_}1092{_}"Oh!
alpsn10{_}1093{_}his name is Cesare," was the answer.
alpsn10{_}1094{_}"Cesare!
alpsn10{_}1095{_}but that is not the name I hear you call him by."
alpsn10{_}1096{_}"Well, perhaps not; we generally call him Cricco," {18} and she looked as if she had suddenly remembered having been told that there were such things as prigs, and might, for aught she knew, be in the presence of one of these creatures now.
alpsn10{_}1097{_}Her husband came to the rescue.
alpsn10{_}1098{_}"Yes," said he, "his real name is Julius Caesar, but we call him Cricco.
alpsn10{_}1099{_}Cricco e un nome di paese; parlando cosi non si offende la religione."
alpsn10{_}1100{_}{19}  The Roman Catholic religion, if left to itself and not compelled to be introspective, is more kindly and less given to taking offence than outsiders generally believe.
alpsn10{_}1101{_}At the Sacro Monte of Varese they sell little round tin boxes that look like medals, and contain pictures of all the chapels.
alpsn10{_}1102{_}In the lid of the box there is a short printed account of the Sacro Monte, which winds up with the words, "La religione e lo stupendo panorama tirano numerosi ed allegri visitatori."
alpsn10{_}1103{_}{20}  Our people are much too earnest to allow that a view could have anything to do with taking people up to the top of a hill where there was a cathedral, or that people could be "merry" while on an errand connected with religion.
alpsn10{_}1104{_}On leaving this place I wanted to say good-bye to Signora Bonvicino, and could not find her; after a time I heard she was at the fountain, so I went and found her on her knees washing her husband's and her own clothes, with her pretty round arms bare nearly to the shoulder.
alpsn10{_}1105{_}It never so much as occurred to her to mind being caught at this work.
alpsn10{_}1106{_}Some months later, shortly before winter, I returned to the same inn for a few days, and found it somewhat demoralised.
alpsn10{_}1107{_}There had been grand doings of some sort, and, though the doings were over, the moral and material debris were not yet quite removed.
alpsn10{_}1108{_}The famiglia Bonvicino was gone, and so was Cricco.
alpsn10{_}1109{_}The cook, the new waiter, and the landlord (who sings a good comic song upon occasion) had all drunk as much wine as they could carry; and later on I found Veneranda, the one-eyed old chambermaid, lying upon my bed fast asleep.
alpsn10{_}1110{_}I afterwards heard that, in spite of the autumnal weather, the landlord spent his night on the grass under the chestnuts, while the cook was found at four o'clock in the morning lying at full length upon a table under the veranda.
alpsn10{_}1111{_}Next day, however, all had become normal again.
alpsn10{_}1112{_}Among our fellow-guests during this visit was a fiery-faced eructive butcher from Turin.
alpsn10{_}1113{_}A difference of opinion having arisen between him and his wife, I told the Signora that I would rather be wrong with her than right with her husband.
alpsn10{_}1114{_}The lady was delighted.
alpsn10{_}1115{_}"Do you hear that, my dear?"
alpsn10{_}1116{_}said she.
alpsn10{_}1117{_}"He says he had rather be wrong with me than right with you.
alpsn10{_}1118{_}Isn't he a naughty man?"
alpsn10{_}1119{_}She said that if she died her husband was going to marry a girl of fifteen.
alpsn10{_}1120{_}I said:  "And if your husband dies, ma'am, send me a dispatch to London, and I will come and marry you myself."
alpsn10{_}1121{_}They were both delighted at this.
alpsn10{_}1122{_}She told us the thunder had upset her and frightened her.
alpsn10{_}1123{_}"Has it given you a headache?"
alpsn10{_}1124{_}She replied:  No; but it had upset her stomach.
alpsn10{_}1125{_}No doubt the thunder had shaken her stomach's confidence in the soundness of its opinions, so as to weaken its proselytising power.
alpsn10{_}1126{_}By and by, seeing that she ate a pretty good dinner, I inquired:  "Is your stomach better now, ma'am?"
alpsn10{_}1127{_}And she said it was.
alpsn10{_}1128{_}Next day my stomach was bad too.
alpsn10{_}1129{_}I told her I had been married, but had lost my wife and had determined never to marry again till I could find a widow whom I had admired as a married woman.
alpsn10{_}1130{_}Giovanni, the new waiter, explained to me that the butcher was not really bad or cruel at all.
alpsn10{_}1131{_}I shook my head at him and said I wished I could think so, but that his poor wife looked very ill and unhappy.
alpsn10{_}1132{_}The housemaid's name was La Rosa Mistica.
alpsn10{_}1133{_}The landlord was a favourite with all the guests.
alpsn10{_}1134{_}Every one patted him on the cheeks or the head, or chucked him under the chin, or did something nice and friendly at him.
alpsn10{_}1135{_}He was a little man with a face like a russet pippin apple, about sixty-five years old, but made of iron.
alpsn10{_}1136{_}He was going to marry a third wife, and six young women had already come up from S. Ambrogio to be looked at.
alpsn10{_}1137{_}I saw one of them.
alpsn10{_}1138{_}She was a Visigoth-looking sort of person and wore a large wobbly-brimmed straw hat; she was about forty, and gave me the impression of being familiar with labour of all kinds.
alpsn10{_}1139{_}He pressed me to give my opinion of her, but I sneaked out of it by declaring that I must see a good deal more of the lady than I was ever likely to see before I could form an opinion at all.
alpsn10{_}1140{_}On coming down from the sanctuary one afternoon I heard the landlord's comic song, of which I have spoken above.
alpsn10{_}1141{_}It was about the musical instruments in a band:  the trumpet did this, the clarinet did that, the flute went tootle, tootle, tootle, and there was an appropriate motion of the hand for every instrument.
alpsn10{_}1142{_}I was a little disappointed with it, but the landlord said I was too serious and the only thing that would cure me was to learn the song myself.
alpsn10{_}1143{_}He said the butcher had learned it already, so it was not hard, which indeed it was not.
alpsn10{_}1144{_}It was about as hard as:   The battle of the Nile I was there all the while At the battle of the Nile.
alpsn10{_}1145{_}I had to learn it and sing it (Heaven help me, for I have no more voice than a mouse!
alpsn10{_}1146{_}), and the landlord said that the motion of my little finger was very promising.
alpsn10{_}1147{_}The chestnuts are never better than after harvest, when they are heavy-laden with their pale green hedgehog-like fruit and alive with people swarming among their branches, pruning them while the leaves are still good winter food for cattle.
alpsn10{_}1148{_}Why, I wonder, is there such an especial charm about the pruning of trees?
alpsn10{_}1149{_}Who does not feel it?
alpsn10{_}1150{_}No matter what the tree is, the poplar of France, or the brookside willow or oak coppice of England, or the chestnuts or mulberries of Italy, all are interesting when being pruned, or when pruned just lately.
alpsn10{_}1151{_}A friend once consulted me casually about a picture on which he was at work, and complained that a row of trees in it was without sufficient interest.
alpsn10{_}1152{_}I was fortunate enough to be able to help him by saying:  "Prune them freely and put a magpie's nest in one of them," and the trees became interesting at once.
alpsn10{_}1153{_}People in trees always look well, or rather, I should say, trees always look well with people in them, or indeed with any living thing in them, especially when it is of a kind that is not commonly seen in them; and the measured lop of the bill-hook and, by and by, the click as a bough breaks and the lazy crash as it falls over on to the ground, are as pleasing to the ear as is the bough-bestrewn herbage to the eye.
alpsn10{_}1154{_}To what height and to what slender boughs do not these hardy climbers trust themselves.
alpsn10{_}1155{_}It is said that the coming man is to be toeless.
alpsn10{_}1156{_}I will venture for it that he will not be toeless if these chestnut-pruning men and women have much to do with his development.
alpsn10{_}1157{_}Let the race prune chestnuts for a couple of hundred generations or so, and it will have little trouble with its toes.
alpsn10{_}1158{_}Of course, the pruners fall sometimes, but very rarely.
alpsn10{_}1159{_}I remember in the Val Mastallone seeing a votive picture of a poor lady in a short petticoat and trousers trimmed with red round the bottom who was falling head foremost from the top of a high tree, whose leaves she had been picking, and was being saved by the intervention of two saints who caught her upon two gridirons.
alpsn10{_}1160{_}Such accidents, however, and, I should think, such interventions, are exceedingly rare, and as a rule the peasants venture freely into places which in England no one but a sailor or a steeple-jack would attempt.
alpsn10{_}1161{_}And so we left this part of Italy, wishing that more Hugo de Montboissiers had committed more crimes and had had to expiate them by building more sanctuaries.
alpsn10{_}1162{_}CHAPTER XI--Lanzo    From S. Ambrogio we went to Turin, a city so well known that I need not describe it.
alpsn10{_}1163{_}The Hotel Europa is the best, and, indeed, one of the best hotels on the continent.
alpsn10{_}1164{_}Nothing can exceed it for comfort and good cookery.
alpsn10{_}1165{_}The gallery of old masters contains some great gems.
alpsn10{_}1166{_}Especially remarkable are two pictures of Tobias and the angel, by Antonio Pollaiuolo and Sandro Botticelli; and a magnificent tempera painting of the Crucifixion, by Gaudenzio Ferrari--one of his very finest works.
alpsn10{_}1167{_}There are also several other pictures by the same master, but the Crucifixion is the best.
alpsn10{_}1168{_}From Turin I went alone to Lanzo, about an hour and a half's railway journey from Turin, and found a comfortable inn, the Hotel de la Poste.
alpsn10{_}1169{_}There is a fine fourteenth-century tower here, and the general effect of the town is good.
alpsn10{_}1170{_}One morning while I was getting my breakfast, English fashion, with some cutlets to accompany my bread and butter, I saw an elderly Italian gentleman, with his hand up to his chin, eyeing me with thoughtful interest.
alpsn10{_}1171{_}After a time he broke silence.
alpsn10{_}1172{_}"Ed il latte," he said, "serve per la suppa."
alpsn10{_}1173{_}{21}  I said that that was the view we took of it.
alpsn10{_}1174{_}He thought it over a while, and then feelingly exclaimed -  "Oh bel!"
alpsn10{_}1175{_}Soon afterwards he left me with the words -  "La!
alpsn10{_}1176{_}dunque!
alpsn10{_}1177{_}cerrea!
alpsn10{_}1178{_}chow!
alpsn10{_}1179{_}stia bene."
alpsn10{_}1180{_}"La" is a very common close to an Italian conversation.
alpsn10{_}1181{_}I used to be a little afraid of it at first.
alpsn10{_}1182{_}It sounds rather like saying, "There, that's that.
alpsn10{_}1183{_}Please to bear in mind that I talked to you very nicely, and let you bore me for a long time; I think I have now done the thing handsomely, so you'll be good enough to score me one and let me go."
alpsn10{_}1184{_}But I soon found out that it was quite a friendly and civil way of saying good-bye.
alpsn10{_}1185{_}The "dunque" is softer; it seems to say, "I cannot bring myself to say so sad a word as 'farewell,' but we must both of us know that the time has come for us to part, and so" -  "Cerrea" is an abbreviation and corruption of "di sua Signoria,"-- "by your highness's leave."
alpsn10{_}1186{_}"Chow" I have explained already.
alpsn10{_}1187{_}"Stia bene" is simply "farewell."
alpsn10{_}1188{_}The principal piazza of Lanzo is nice.
alpsn10{_}1189{_}In the upper part of the town there is a large school or college.
alpsn10{_}1190{_}One can see into the school through a grating from the road.
alpsn10{_}1191{_}I looked down, and saw that the boys had cut their names all over the desks, just as English boys would do.
alpsn10{_}1192{_}They were very merry and noisy, and though there was a priest standing at one end of the room, he let them do much as they liked, and they seemed quite happy.
alpsn10{_}1193{_}I heard one boy shout out to another, "Non c' e pericolo," in answer to something the other had said.
alpsn10{_}1194{_}This is exactly the "no fear" of America and the colonies.
alpsn10{_}1195{_}Near the school there is a field on the slope of the hill which commands a view over the plain.
alpsn10{_}1196{_}A woman was mowing there, and, by way of making myself agreeable, I remarked that the view was fine.
alpsn10{_}1197{_}"Yes, it is," she answered; "you can see all the trains."
alpsn10{_}1198{_}The baskets with which the people carry things in this neighbourhood are of a different construction from any I have seen elsewhere.
alpsn10{_}1199{_}They are made to fit all round the head like something between a saddle and a helmet, and at the same time to rest upon the shoulders--the head being, as it were, ensaddled by the basket, and the weight being supported by the shoulders as well as by the head.
alpsn10{_}1200{_}Why is it that such contrivances as this should prevail in one valley and not in another?
alpsn10{_}1201{_}If, one is tempted to argue, the plan is a convenient one, why does it not spread further?
alpsn10{_}1202{_}If inconvenient, why has it spread so far?
alpsn10{_}1203{_}If it is good in the valley of the Stura, why is it not also good in the contiguous valley of the Dora?
alpsn10{_}1204{_}There must be places where people using helmet-made baskets live next door to people who use baskets that are borne entirely by back and shoulders.
alpsn10{_}1205{_}Why do not the people in one or other of these houses adopt their neighbour's basket?
alpsn10{_}1206{_}Not because people are not amenable to conviction, for within a certain radius from the source of the invention they are convinced to a man.
alpsn10{_}1207{_}Nor again is it from any insuperable objection to a change of habit.
alpsn10{_}1208{_}The Stura people have changed their habit--possibly for the worse; but if they have changed it for the worse, how is it they do not find it out and change again?
alpsn10{_}1209{_}Take, again, the pane Grissino, from which the neighbourhood of Turin has derived its nickname of il Grissinotto.
alpsn10{_}1210{_}It is made in long sticks, rather thicker than a tobacco pipe, and eats crisp like toast.
alpsn10{_}1211{_}It is almost universally preferred to ordinary bread by the inhabitants of what was formerly Piedmont, but beyond these limits it is rarely seen.
alpsn10{_}1212{_}Why so?
alpsn10{_}1213{_}Either it is good or not good.
alpsn10{_}1214{_}If not good, how has it prevailed over so large an area?
alpsn10{_}1215{_}If good, why does it not extend its empire?
alpsn10{_}1216{_}The Reformation is another case in point:  granted that Protestantism is illogical, how is it that so few within a given area can perceive it to be so?
alpsn10{_}1217{_}The same question arises in respect of the distribution of many plants and animals; the reason of the limits which some of them cannot pass, being, indeed, perfectly clear, but as regards perhaps the greater number of them, undiscoverable.
alpsn10{_}1218{_}The upshot of it is that things do not in practice find their perfect level any more than water does so, but are liable to disturbance by way of tides and local currents, or storms.
alpsn10{_}1219{_}It is in his power to perceive and profit by these irregularities that the strength or weakness of a commercial man will be apparent,  One day I made an excursion from Lanzo to a place, the name of which I cannot remember, but which is not far from the Groscavallo glacier.
alpsn10{_}1220{_}Here I found several Italians staying to take the air, and among them one young gentleman, who told me he was writing a book upon this neighbourhood, and was going to illustrate it with his own drawings.
alpsn10{_}1221{_}This naturally interested me, and I encouraged him to tell me more, which he was nothing loth to do.
alpsn10{_}1222{_}He said he had a passion for drawing, and was making rapid progress; but there was one thing that held him back--the not having any Conte chalk: if he had but this, all his difficulties would vanish.
alpsn10{_}1223{_}Unfortunately I had no Conte chalk with me, I but I asked to see the drawings, and was shown about twenty, all of which greatly pleased me.
alpsn10{_}1224{_}I at once proposed an exchange, and have thus become possessed of the two which I reproduce here.
alpsn10{_}1225{_}Being pencil drawings, and not done with a view to Mr. Dawson's process, they have suffered somewhat in reproduction, but I decided to let them suffer rather than attempt to copy them.
alpsn10{_}1226{_}What can be more absolutely in the spirit of the fourteenth century than the drawings given above?
alpsn10{_}1227{_}They seem as though done by some fourteenth- century painter who had risen from the dead.
alpsn10{_}1228{_}And to show that they are no rare accident, I will give another (p. 138), also done by an entirely self-taught Italian, and intended to represent the castle of Laurenzana in the neighbourhood of Potenza.
alpsn10{_}1229{_}If the reader will pardon a digression, I will refer to a more important example of an old master born out of due time.
alpsn10{_}1230{_}One day, in the cathedral at Varallo, I saw a picture painted on linen of which I could make nothing.
alpsn10{_}1231{_}It was not old and it was not modern.
alpsn10{_}1232{_}The expression of the Virgin's face was lovely, and there was more individuality than is commonly found in modern Italian work.
alpsn10{_}1233{_}Modern Italian colour is generally either cold and dirty, or else staring.
alpsn10{_}1234{_}The colour here was tender, and reminded me of fifteenth- century Florentine work.
alpsn10{_}1235{_}The folds of the drapery were not modern; there was a sense of effort about them, as though the painter had tried to do them better, but had been unable to get them as free and flowing as he had wished.
alpsn10{_}1236{_}Yet the picture was not old; to all appearance it might have been painted a matter of ten years; nor again was it an echo--it was a sound:  the archaism was not affected; on the contrary, there was something which said, as plainly as though the living painter had spoken it, that his somewhat constrained treatment was due simply to his having been puzzled with the intricacy of what he saw, and giving as much as he could with a hand which was less advanced than his judgment.
alpsn10{_}1237{_}By some strange law it comes about that the imperfection of men who are at this stage of any art is the only true perfection; for the wisdom of the wise is set at naught, and the foolishness of the simple is chosen, and it is out of the mouths of babes and sucklings that strength is ordained.
alpsn10{_}1238{_}Unable to arrive at any conclusion, I asked the sacristan, and was told it was by a certain Dedomenici of Rossa, in the Val Sesia, and that it had been painted some forty or fifty years ago.
alpsn10{_}1239{_}I expressed my surprise, and the sacristan continued:  "Yes, but what is most wonderful about him is that he never left his native valley, and never had any instruction, but picked up his art for himself as best he could."
alpsn10{_}1240{_}I have been twice to Varallo since, to see whether I should change my mind, but have not done so.
alpsn10{_}1241{_}If Dedomenici had been a Florentine or Venetian in the best times, he would have done as well as the best; as it is, his work is remarkable.
alpsn10{_}1242{_}He died about 1840, very old, and he kept on improving to the last.
alpsn10{_}1243{_}His last work--at least I was told upon the spot that it was his last--is in a little roadside chapel perched high upon a rock, and dedicated, if I remember rightly, to S. Michele, on the path from Fobello in the Val Mastallone to Taponaccio.
alpsn10{_}1244{_}It is a Madonna and child in clouds, with two full-length saints standing beneath--all the figures life- size.
alpsn10{_}1245{_}I came upon this chapel quite accidentally one evening, and, looking in, recognised the altar-piece as a Dedomenici.
alpsn10{_}1246{_}I inquired at the next village who had painted it, and was told, "un certo Dedomenici da Rossa."
alpsn10{_}1247{_}I was also told that he was nearly eighty years old when he painted this picture.
alpsn10{_}1248{_}I went a couple of years ago to reconsider it, and found that I remained much of my original opinion.
alpsn10{_}1249{_}I do not think that any of my readers who care about the history of Italian art will regret having paid it a visit.
alpsn10{_}1250{_}Such men are more common in Italy than is believed.
alpsn10{_}1251{_}There is a fresco of the Crucifixion outside the Campo Santo at Fusio, in the Canton Ticino, done by a local artist, which, though far inferior to the work of Dedomenici, is still remarkable.
alpsn10{_}1252{_}The painter evidently knows nothing of the rules of his art, but he has made Christ on the cross bowing His head towards the souls in purgatory, instead of in the conventional fine frenzy to which we are accustomed.
alpsn10{_}1253{_}There is a storm which has caught and is sweeping the drapery round Christ's body.
alpsn10{_}1254{_}The angel's wings are no longer white, but many coloured as in old times, and there is a touch of humour in the fact that of the six souls in purgatory, four are women and only two men.
alpsn10{_}1255{_}The expression on Christ's face is very fine, but otherwise the drawing could not well be more imperfect than it is.
alpsn10{_}1256{_}CHAPTER XII--Considerations on the Decline of Italian Art    Those who know the Italians will see no sign of decay about them.
alpsn10{_}1257{_}They are the quickest witted people in the world, and at the same time have much more of the old Roman steadiness than they are generally credited with.
alpsn10{_}1258{_}Not only is there no sign of degeneration, but, as regards practical matters, there is every sign of health and vigorous development.
alpsn10{_}1259{_}The North Italians are more like Englishmen, both in body and mind, than any other people whom I know; I am continually meeting Italians whom I should take for Englishmen if I did not know their nationality.
alpsn10{_}1260{_}They have all our strong points, but they have more grace and elasticity of mind than we have.
alpsn10{_}1261{_}Priggishness is the sin which doth most easily beset middle-class and so-called educated Englishmen:  we call it purity and culture, but it does not much matter what we call it.
alpsn10{_}1262{_}It is the almost inevitable outcome of a university education, and will last as long as Oxford and Cambridge do, but not much longer.
alpsn10{_}1263{_}Lord Beaconsfield sent Lothair to Oxford; it is with great pleasure that I see he did not send Endymion.
alpsn10{_}1264{_}My friend Jones called my attention to this, and we noted that the growth observable throughout Lord Beaconsfield's life was continued to the end.
alpsn10{_}1265{_}He was one of those who, no matter how long he lived, would have been always growing:  this is what makes his later novels so much better than those of Thackeray or Dickens.
alpsn10{_}1266{_}There was something of the child about him to the last.
alpsn10{_}1267{_}Earnestness was his greatest danger, but if he did not quite overcome it (as who indeed can?
alpsn10{_}1268{_}It is the last enemy that shall be subdued), he managed to veil it with a fair amount of success.
alpsn10{_}1269{_}As for Endymion, of course if Lord Beaconsfield had thought Oxford would be good for him, he could, as Jones pointed out to me, just as well have killed Mr. Ferrars a year or two later.
alpsn10{_}1270{_}We feel satisfied, therefore, that Endymion's exclusion from a university was carefully considered, and are glad.
alpsn10{_}1271{_}I will not say that priggishness is absolutely unknown among the North Italians; sometimes one comes upon a young Italian who wants to learn German, but not often.
alpsn10{_}1272{_}Priggism, or whatever the substantive is, is as essentially a Teutonic vice as holiness is a Semitic characteristic; and if an Italian happens to be a prig, he will, like Tacitus, invariably show a hankering after German institutions.
alpsn10{_}1273{_}The idea, however, that the Italians were ever a finer people than they are now, will not pass muster with those who know them.
alpsn10{_}1274{_}At the same time, there can be no doubt that modern Italian art is in many respects as bad as it was once good.
alpsn10{_}1275{_}I will confine myself to painting only.
alpsn10{_}1276{_}The modern Italian painters, with very few exceptions, paint as badly as we do, or even worse, and their motives are as poor as is their painting.
alpsn10{_}1277{_}At an exhibition of modern Italian pictures, I generally feel that there is hardly a picture on the walls but is a sham--that is to say, painted not from love of this particular subject and an irresistible desire to paint it, but from a wish to paint an academy picture, and win money or applause.
alpsn10{_}1278{_}The same holds good in England, and in all other countries that I know of.
alpsn10{_}1279{_}There is very little tolerable painting anywhere.
alpsn10{_}1280{_}In some kinds, indeed, of black and white work the present age is strong.
alpsn10{_}1281{_}The illustrations to "Punch," for example, are often as good as anything that can be imagined.
alpsn10{_}1282{_}We know of nothing like them in any past age or country.
alpsn10{_}1283{_}This is the one kind of art--and it is a very good one--in which we excel as distinctly as the age of Phidias excelled in sculpture.
alpsn10{_}1284{_}Leonardo da Vinci would never have succeeded in getting his drawings accepted at 85 Fleet Street, any more than one of the artists on the staff of "Punch" could paint a fresco which should hold its own against Da Vinci's Last Supper.
alpsn10{_}1285{_}Michael Angelo again and Titian would have failed disastrously at modern illustration.
alpsn10{_}1286{_}They had no more sense of humour than a Hebrew prophet; they had no eye for the more trivial side of anything round about them.
alpsn10{_}1287{_}This aspect went in at one eye and out at the other--and they lost more than ever poor Peter Bell lost in the matter of primroses.
alpsn10{_}1288{_}I never can see what there was to find fault with in that young man.
alpsn10{_}1289{_}Fancy a street-Arab by Michael Angelo.
alpsn10{_}1290{_}Fancy even the result which would have ensued if he had tried to put the figures into the illustrations of this book.
alpsn10{_}1291{_}I should have been very sorry to let him try his hand at it.
alpsn10{_}1292{_}To him a priest chucking a small boy under the chin was simply non-existent.
alpsn10{_}1293{_}He did not care for it, and had therefore no eye for it.
alpsn10{_}1294{_}If the reader will turn to the copy of a fresco of St. Christopher on p. 209, he will see the conventional treatment of the rocks on either side the saint.
alpsn10{_}1295{_}This was the best thing the artist could do, and probably cost him no little trouble.
alpsn10{_}1296{_}Yet there were rocks all around him--little, in fact, else than rock in those days; and the artist could have drawn them well enough if it had occurred to him to try and do so.
alpsn10{_}1297{_}If he could draw St. Christopher, he could have drawn a rock; but he had an interest in the one, and saw nothing in the other which made him think it worth while to pay attention to it.
alpsn10{_}1298{_}What rocks were to him, the common occurrences of everyday life were to those who are generally held to be the giants of painting.
alpsn10{_}1299{_}The result of this neglect to kiss the soil--of this attempt to be always soaring--is that these giants are for the most part now very uninteresting, while the smaller men who preceded them grow fresher and more delightful yearly.
alpsn10{_}1300{_}It was not so with Handel and Shakespeare.
alpsn10{_}1301{_}Handel's   "Ploughman near at hand, whistling o'er the furrowed land,"   is intensely sympathetic, and his humour is admirable whenever he has occasion for it.
alpsn10{_}1302{_}Leonardo da Vinci is the only one of the giant Italian masters who ever tried to be humorous, and he failed completely:  so, indeed, must any one if he tries to be humorous.
alpsn10{_}1303{_}We do not want this; we only want them not to shut their eyes to by-play when it comes in their way, and if they are giving us an account of what they have seen, to tell us something about this too.
alpsn10{_}1304{_}I believe the older the world grows, the better it enjoys a joke.
alpsn10{_}1305{_}The mediaeval joke generally was a heavy, lumbering old thing, only a little better than the classical one.
alpsn10{_}1306{_}Perhaps in those days life was harder than it is now, and people if they looked at it at all closely dwelt upon its soberer side.
alpsn10{_}1307{_}Certainly in humorous art, we may claim to be not only principes, but facile principes.
alpsn10{_}1308{_}Nevertheless, the Italian comic journals are, some of them, admirably illustrated, though in a style quite different from our own; sometimes, also, they are beautifully coloured.
alpsn10{_}1309{_}As regards painting, the last rays of the sunset of genuine art are to be found in the votive pictures at Locarno or Oropa, and in many a wayside chapel.
alpsn10{_}1310{_}In these, religious art still lingers as a living language, however rudely spoken.
alpsn10{_}1311{_}In these alone is the story told, not as in the Latin and Greek verses of the scholar, who thinks he has succeeded best when he has most concealed his natural manner of expressing himself, but by one who knows what he wants to say, and says it in his mother-tongue, shortly, and without caring whether or not his words are in accordance with academic rules.
alpsn10{_}1312{_}I regret to see photography being introduced for votive purposes, and also to detect in some places a disposition on the part of the authorities to be a little ashamed of these pictures and to place them rather out of sight.
alpsn10{_}1313{_}Sometimes in a little country village, as at Doera near Mesocco, there is a modern fresco on a chapel in which the old spirit appears, with its absolute indifference as to whether it was ridiculous or no, but such examples are rare.
alpsn10{_}1314{_}Sometimes, again, I have even thought I have detected a ray of sunset upon a milkman's window-blind in London, and once upon an undertaker's, but it was too faint a ray to read by.
alpsn10{_}1315{_}The best thing of the kind that I have seen in London is the picture of the lady who is cleaning knives with Mr. Spong's patent knife-cleaner, in his shop window nearly opposite Day & Martin's in Holborn.
alpsn10{_}1316{_}It falls a long way short, however, of a good Italian votive picture: but it has the advantage of moving.
alpsn10{_}1317{_}I knew of a little girl once, rather less than four years old, whose uncle had promised to take her for a drive in a carriage with him, and had failed to do so.
alpsn10{_}1318{_}The child was found soon afterwards on the stairs weeping, and being asked what was the matter, replied, "Mans is all alike."
alpsn10{_}1319{_}This is Giottesque.
alpsn10{_}1320{_}I often think of it as I look upon Italian votive pictures.
alpsn10{_}1321{_}The meaning is so sound in spite of the expression being so defective--if, indeed, expression can be defective when it has so well conveyed the meaning.
alpsn10{_}1322{_}I knew, again, an old lady whose education had been neglected in her youth.
alpsn10{_}1323{_}She came into a large fortune, and at some forty years of age put herself under the best masters.
alpsn10{_}1324{_}She once said to me as follows, speaking very slowly and allowing a long time between each part of the sentence;--"You see," she said, "the world, and all that it contains, is wrapped up in such curious forms, that it is only by a knowledge of human nature, that we can rightly tell what to say, to do, or to admire."
alpsn10{_}1325{_}I copied the sentence into my notebook immediately on taking my leave.
alpsn10{_}1326{_}It is like an academy picture.
alpsn10{_}1327{_}But to return to the Italians.
alpsn10{_}1328{_}The question is, how has the deplorable falling-off in Italian painting been caused?
alpsn10{_}1329{_}And by doing what may we again get Bellinis and Andrea Mantegnas as in old time?
alpsn10{_}1330{_}The fault does not lie in any want of raw material:  the drawings I have already given prove this.
alpsn10{_}1331{_}Nor, again, does it lie in want of taking pains.
alpsn10{_}1332{_}The modern Italian painter frets himself to the full as much as his predecessor did--if the truth were known, probably a great deal more.
alpsn10{_}1333{_}It does not lie in want of schooling or art education.
alpsn10{_}1334{_}For the last three hundred years, ever since the Carracci opened their academy at Bologna, there has been no lack of art education in Italy.
alpsn10{_}1335{_}Curiously enough, the date of the opening of the Bolognese Academy coincides as nearly as may be with the complete decadence of Italian painting.
alpsn10{_}1336{_}This is an example of the way in which Italian boys begin their art education now.
alpsn10{_}1337{_}The drawing which I reproduce here was given me by the eminent sculptor, Professor Vela, as the work of a lad of twelve years old, and as doing credit alike to the school where the lad was taught and to the pupil himself.
alpsn10{_}1338{_}{22}  So it undoubtedly does.
alpsn10{_}1339{_}It shows as plainly the receptiveness and docility of the modern Italian, as the illustrations given above show his freshness and naivete when left to himself.
alpsn10{_}1340{_}The drawing is just such as we try to get our own young people to do, and few English elementary schools in a small country town would succeed in turning out so good a one.
alpsn10{_}1341{_}I have nothing, therefore, but praise both for the pupil and the teacher; but about the system which makes such teachers and such pupils commendable, I am more sceptical.
alpsn10{_}1342{_}That system trains boys to study other people's works rather than nature, and, as Leonardo da Vinci so well says, it makes them nature's grandchildren and not her children.
alpsn10{_}1343{_}The boy who did the drawing given above is not likely to produce good work in later life.
alpsn10{_}1344{_}He has been taught to see nature with an old man's eyes at once, without going through the embryonic stages.
alpsn10{_}1345{_}He has never said his "mans is all alike," and by twenty will be painting like my old friend's long academic sentence.
alpsn10{_}1346{_}All his individuality has been crushed out of him.
alpsn10{_}1347{_}I will now give a reproduction of the frontispiece to Avogadro's work on the sanctuary of S. Michele, from which I have already quoted; it is a very pretty and effective piece of work, but those who are good enough to turn back to p. 93, and to believe that I have drawn carefully, will see how disappointing Avogadro's frontispiece must be to those who hold, as most of us will, that a draughtsman's first business is to put down what he sees, and to let prettiness take care of itself.
alpsn10{_}1348{_}The main features, indeed, can still be traced, but they have become as transformed and lifeless as rudimentary organs.
alpsn10{_}1349{_}Such a frontispiece, however, is the almost inevitable consequence of the system of training that will make boys of twelve do drawings like the one given on p. 147.
alpsn10{_}1350{_}If half a dozen young Italians could be got together with a taste for drawing like that shown by the authors of the sketches on pp.
alpsn10{_}1351{_}136, 137, 138; if they had power to add to their number; if they were allowed to see paintings and drawings done up to the year A.D. 1510, and votive pictures and the comic papers; if they were left with no other assistance than this, absolutely free to please themselves, and could be persuaded not to try and please any one else, I believe that in fifty years we should have all that was ever done repeated with fresh naivete, and as much more delightfully than even by the best old masters, as these are more delightful than anything we know of in classic painting.
alpsn10{_}1352{_}The young plants keep growing up abundantly every day--look at Bastianini, dead not ten years since--but they are browsed down by the academies.
alpsn10{_}1353{_}I remember there came out a book many years ago with the title, "What becomes of all the clever little children?"
alpsn10{_}1354{_}I never saw the book, but the title is pertinent.
alpsn10{_}1355{_}Any man who can write, can draw to a not inconsiderable extent.
alpsn10{_}1356{_}Look at the Bayeux tapestry; yet Matilda probably never had a drawing lesson in her life.
alpsn10{_}1357{_}See how well prisoner after prisoner in the Tower of London has cut this or that out in the stone of his prison wall, without, in all probability, having ever tried his hand at drawing before.
alpsn10{_}1358{_}Look at my friend Jones, who has several illustrations in this book.
alpsn10{_}1359{_}The first year he went abroad with me he could hardly draw at all.
alpsn10{_}1360{_}He was no year away from England more than three weeks.
alpsn10{_}1361{_}How did he learn?
alpsn10{_}1362{_}On the old principle, if I am not mistaken.
alpsn10{_}1363{_}The old principle was for a man to be doing something which he was pretty strongly bent on doing, and to get a much younger one to help him.
alpsn10{_}1364{_}The younger paid nothing for instruction, but the elder took the work, as long as the relation of master and pupil existed between them.
alpsn10{_}1365{_}I, then, was making illustrations for this book, and got Jones to help me.
alpsn10{_}1366{_}I let him see what I was doing, and derive an idea of the sort of thing I wanted, and then left him alone--beyond giving him the same kind of small criticism that I expected from himself--but I appropriated his work.
alpsn10{_}1367{_}That is the way to teach, and the result was that in an incredibly short time Jones could draw.
alpsn10{_}1368{_}The taking the work is a sine qua non.
alpsn10{_}1369{_}If I had not been going to have his work, Jones, in spite of all his quickness, would probably have been rather slower in learning to draw.
alpsn10{_}1370{_}Being paid in money is nothing like so good.
alpsn10{_}1371{_}This is the system of apprenticeship versus the academic system.
alpsn10{_}1372{_}The academic system consists in giving people the rules for doing things.
alpsn10{_}1373{_}The apprenticeship system consists in letting them do it, with just a trifle of supervision.
alpsn10{_}1374{_}"For all a rhetorician's rules," says my great namesake, "teach nothing, but to name his tools;" and academic rules generally are much the same as the rhetorician's.
alpsn10{_}1375{_}Some men can pass through academies unscathed, but they are very few, and in the main the academic influence is a baleful one, whether exerted in a university or a school.
alpsn10{_}1376{_}While young men at universities are being prepared for their entry into life, their rivals have already entered it.
alpsn10{_}1377{_}The most university and examination ridden people in the world are the Chinese, and they are the least progressive.
alpsn10{_}1378{_}Men should learn to draw as they learn conveyancing:  they should go into a painter's studio and paint on his pictures.
alpsn10{_}1379{_}I am told that half the conveyances in the country are drawn by pupils; there is no more mystery about painting than about conveyancing--not half in fact, I should think, so much.
alpsn10{_}1380{_}One may ask, How can the beginner paint, or draw conveyances, till he has learnt how to do so?
alpsn10{_}1381{_}The answer is, How can he learn, without at any rate trying to do?
alpsn10{_}1382{_}If he likes his subject, he will try:  if he tries, he will soon succeed in doing something which shall open a door.
alpsn10{_}1383{_}It does not matter what a man does; so long as he does it with the attention which affection engenders, he will come to see his way to something else.
alpsn10{_}1384{_}After long waiting he will certainly find one door open, and go through it.
alpsn10{_}1385{_}He will say to himself that he can never find another.
alpsn10{_}1386{_}He has found this, more by luck than cunning, but now he is done.
alpsn10{_}1387{_}Yet by and by he will see that there is ONE more small, unimportant door which he had overlooked, and he proceeds through this too.
alpsn10{_}1388{_}If he remains now for a long while and sees no other, do not let him fret; doors are like the kingdom of heaven, they come not by observation, least of all do they come by forcing: let them just go on doing what comes nearest, but doing it attentively, and a great wide door will one day spring into existence where there had been no sign of one but a little time previously.
alpsn10{_}1389{_}Only let him be always doing something, and let him cross himself now and again, for belief in the wondrous efficacy of crosses and crossing is the corner-stone of the creed of the evolutionist.
alpsn10{_}1390{_}Then after years--but not probably till after a great many--doors will open up all round, so many and so wide that the difficulty will not be to find a door, but rather to obtain the means of even hurriedly surveying a portion of those that stand invitingly open.
alpsn10{_}1391{_}I know that just as good a case can be made out for the other side.
alpsn10{_}1392{_}It may be said as truly that unless a student is incessantly on the watch for doors he will never see them, and that unless he is incessantly pressing forward to the kingdom of heaven he will never find it--so that the kingdom does come by observation.
alpsn10{_}1393{_}It is with this as with everything else--there must be a harmonious fusing of two principles which are in flat contradiction to one another.
alpsn10{_}1394{_}The question whether it is better to abide quiet and take advantage of opportunities that come, or to go further afield in search of them, is one of the oldest which living beings have had to deal with.
alpsn10{_}1395{_}It was on this that the first great schism or heresy arose in what was heretofore the catholic faith of protoplasm.
alpsn10{_}1396{_}The schism still lasts, and has resulted in two great sects--animals and plants.
alpsn10{_}1397{_}The opinion that it is better to go in search of prey is formulated in animals; the other--that it is better on the whole to stay at home and profit by what comes--in plants.
alpsn10{_}1398{_}Some intermediate forms still record to us the long struggle during which the schism was not yet complete.
alpsn10{_}1399{_}If I may be pardoned for pursuing this digression further, I would say that it is the plants and not we who are the heretics.
alpsn10{_}1400{_}There can be no question about this; we are perfectly justified, therefore, in devouring them.
alpsn10{_}1401{_}Ours is the original and orthodox belief, for protoplasm is much more animal than vegetable; it is much more true to say that plants have descended from animals than animals from plants.
alpsn10{_}1402{_}Nevertheless, like many other heretics, plants have thriven very fairly well.
alpsn10{_}1403{_}There are a great many of them, and as regards beauty, if not wit--of a limited kind indeed, but still wit--it is hard to say that the animal kingdom has the advantage.
alpsn10{_}1404{_}The views of plants are sadly narrow; all dissenters are narrow-minded; but within their own bounds they know the details of their business sufficiently well--as well as though they kept the most nicely-balanced system of accounts to show them their position.
alpsn10{_}1405{_}They are eaten, it is true; to eat them is our bigoted and intolerant way of trying to convert them:  eating is only a violent mode of proselytising or converting; and we do convert them--to good animal substance, of our own way of thinking.
alpsn10{_}1406{_}But then, animals are eaten too.
alpsn10{_}1407{_}They convert one another, almost as much as they convert plants.
alpsn10{_}1408{_}And an animal is no sooner dead than a plant will convert it back again.
alpsn10{_}1409{_}It is obvious, however, that no schism could have been so long successful, without having a good deal to say for itself.
alpsn10{_}1410{_}Neither party has been quite consistent.
alpsn10{_}1411{_}Who ever is or can be?
alpsn10{_}1412{_}Every extreme--every opinion carried to its logical end--will prove to be an absurdity.
alpsn10{_}1413{_}Plants throw out roots and boughs and leaves; this is a kind of locomotion; and as Dr. Erasmus Darwin long since pointed out, they do sometimes approach nearly to what may be called travelling; a man of consistent character will never look at a bough, a root, or a tendril without regarding it as a melancholy and unprincipled compromise.
alpsn10{_}1414{_}On the other hand, many animals are sessile, and some singularly successful genera, as spiders, are in the main liers-in-wait.
alpsn10{_}1415{_}It may appear, however, on the whole, like reopening a settled question to uphold the principle of being busy and attentive over a small area, rather than going to and fro over a larger one, for a mammal like man, but I think most readers will be with me in thinking that, at any rate as regards art and literature, it is he who does his small immediate work most carefully who will find doors open most certainly to him, that will conduct him into the richest chambers.
alpsn10{_}1416{_}Many years ago, in New Zealand, I used sometimes to accompany a dray and team of bullocks who would have to be turned loose at night that they might feed.
alpsn10{_}1417{_}There were no hedges or fences then, so sometimes I could not find my team in the morning, and had no clue to the direction in which they had gone.
alpsn10{_}1418{_}At first I used to try and throw my soul into the bullocks' souls, so as to divine if possible what they would be likely to have done, and would then ride off ten miles in the wrong direction.
alpsn10{_}1419{_}People used in those days to lose their bullocks sometimes for a week or fortnight--when they perhaps were all the time hiding in a gully hard by the place where they were turned out.
alpsn10{_}1420{_}After some time I changed my tactics.
alpsn10{_}1421{_}On losing my bullocks I would go to the nearest accommodation house, and stand occasional drinks to travellers.
alpsn10{_}1422{_}Some one would ere long, as a general rule, turn up who had seen the bullocks.
alpsn10{_}1423{_}This case does not go quite on all fours with what I have been saying above, inasmuch as I was not very industrious in my limited area; but the standing drinks and inquiring was being as industrious as the circumstances would allow.
alpsn10{_}1424{_}To return, universities and academies are an obstacle to the finding of doors in later life; partly because they push their young men too fast through doorways that the universities have provided, and so discourage the habit of being on the look-out for others; and partly because they do not take pains enough to make sure that their doors are bona fide ones.
alpsn10{_}1425{_}If, to change the metaphor, an academy has taken a bad shilling, it is seldom very scrupulous about trying to pass it on.
alpsn10{_}1426{_}It will stick to it that the shilling is a good one as long as the police will let it.
alpsn10{_}1427{_}I was very happy at Cambridge; when I left it I thought I never again could be so happy anywhere else; I shall ever retain a most kindly recollection both of Cambridge and of the school where I passed my boyhood; but I feel, as I think most others must in middle life, that I have spent as much of my maturer years in unlearning as in learning.
alpsn10{_}1428{_}The proper course is for a boy to begin the practical business of life many years earlier than he now commonly does.
alpsn10{_}1429{_}He should begin at the very bottom of a profession; if possible of one which his family has pursued before him--for the professions will assuredly one day become hereditary.
alpsn10{_}1430{_}The ideal railway director will have begun at fourteen as a railway porter.
alpsn10{_}1431{_}He need not be a porter for more than a week or ten days, any more than he need have been a tadpole more than a short time; but he should take a turn in practice, though briefly, at each of the lower branches in the profession.
alpsn10{_}1432{_}The painter should do just the same.
alpsn10{_}1433{_}He should begin by setting his employer's palette and cleaning his brushes.
alpsn10{_}1434{_}As for the good side of universities, the proper preservative of this is to be found in the club.
alpsn10{_}1435{_}If, then, we are to have a renaissance of art, there must be a complete standing aloof from the academic system.
alpsn10{_}1436{_}That system has had time enough.
alpsn10{_}1437{_}Where and who are its men?
alpsn10{_}1438{_}Can it point to one painter who can hold his own with the men of, say, from 1450 to 1550?
alpsn10{_}1439{_}Academies will bring out men who can paint hair very like hair, and eyes very like eyes, but this is not enough.
alpsn10{_}1440{_}This is grammar and deportment; we want it and a kindly nature, and these cannot be got from academies.
alpsn10{_}1441{_}As far as mere TECHNIQUE is concerned, almost every one now can paint as well as is in the least desirable.
alpsn10{_}1442{_}The same mutatis mutandis holds good with writing as with painting.
alpsn10{_}1443{_}We want less word-painting and fine phrases, and more observation at first-hand.
alpsn10{_}1444{_}Let us have a periodical illustrated by people who cannot draw, and written by people who cannot write (perhaps, however, after all, we have some), but who look and think for themselves, and express themselves just as they please,--and this we certainly have not.
alpsn10{_}1445{_}Every contributor should be at once turned out if he or she is generally believed to have tried to do something which he or she did not care about trying to do, and anything should be admitted which is the outcome of a genuine liking.
alpsn10{_}1446{_}People are always good company when they are doing what they really enjoy.
alpsn10{_}1447{_}A cat is good company when it is purring, or a dog when it is wagging its tail.
alpsn10{_}1448{_}The sketching clubs up and down the country might form the nucleus of such a society, provided all professional men were rigorously excluded.
alpsn10{_}1449{_}As for the old masters, the better plan would be never even to look at one of them, and to consign Raffaelle, along with Plato, Marcus Aurelius Antoninus, Dante, Goethe, and two others, neither of them Englishmen, to limbo, as the Seven Humbugs of Christendom.
alpsn10{_}1450{_}While we are about it, let us leave off talking about "art for art's sake."
alpsn10{_}1451{_}Who is art that it should have a sake?
alpsn10{_}1452{_}A work of art should be produced for the pleasure it gives the producer, and the pleasure he thinks it will give to a few of whom he is fond; but neither money nor people whom he does not know personally should be thought of.
alpsn10{_}1453{_}Of course such a society as I have proposed would not remain incorrupt long.
alpsn10{_}1454{_}"Everything that grows, holds in perfection but a little moment."
alpsn10{_}1455{_}The members would try to imitate professional men in spite of their rules, or, if they escaped this and after a while got to paint well, they would become dogmatic, and a rebellion against their authority would be as necessary ere long as it was against that of their predecessors:  but the balance on the whole would be to the good.
alpsn10{_}1456{_}Professional men should be excluded, if for no other reason yet for this, that they know too much for the beginner to be en rapport with them.
alpsn10{_}1457{_}It is the beginner who can help the beginner, as it is the child who is the most instructive companion for another child.
alpsn10{_}1458{_}The beginner can understand the beginner, but the cross between him and the proficient performer is too wide for fertility.
alpsn10{_}1459{_}It savours of impatience, and is in flat contradiction to the first principles of biology.
alpsn10{_}1460{_}It does a beginner positive harm to look at the masterpieces of the great executionists, such as Rembrandt or Turner.
alpsn10{_}1461{_}If one is climbing a very high mountain which will tax all one's strength, nothing fatigues so much as casting upward glances to the top, nothing encourages so much as casting downward glances.
alpsn10{_}1462{_}The top seems never to draw nearer; the parts that we have passed retreat rapidly.
alpsn10{_}1463{_}Let a water-colour student go and see the drawing by Turner, in the basement of our National Gallery, dated 1787.
alpsn10{_}1464{_}This is the sort of thing for him, not to copy, but to look at for a minute or two now and again.
alpsn10{_}1465{_}It will show him nothing about painting, but it may serve to teach him not to overtax his strength, and will prove to him that the greatest masters in painting, as in everything else, begin by doing work which is no way superior to that of their neighbours.
alpsn10{_}1466{_}A collection of the earliest known works of the greatest men would be much more useful to the student than any number of their maturer works, for it would show him that he need not worry himself because his work does not look clever, or as silly people say, "show power."
alpsn10{_}1467{_}The secrets of success are affection for the pursuit chosen, a flat refusal to be hurried or to pass anything as understood which is not understood, and an obstinacy of character which shall make the student's friends find it less trouble to let him have his own way than to bend him into theirs.
alpsn10{_}1468{_}Our schools and academies or universities are covertly, but essentially, radical institutions and abhorrent to the genius of Conservatism.
alpsn10{_}1469{_}Their sin is the true radical sin of being in too great a hurry, and of believing in short cuts too soon.
alpsn10{_}1470{_}But it must be remembered that this proposition, like every other, wants tempering with a slight infusion of its direct opposite.
alpsn10{_}1471{_}I said in an early part of this book that the best test to know whether or no one likes a picture is to ask one's self whether one would like to look at it if one was quite sure one was alone.
alpsn10{_}1472{_}The best test for a painter as to whether he likes painting his picture is to ask himself whether he should like to paint it if he was quite sure that no one except himself, and the few of whom he was very fond, would ever see it.
alpsn10{_}1473{_}If he can answer this question in the affirmative, he is all right; if he cannot, he is all wrong.
alpsn10{_}1474{_}I will close these remarks with an illustration which will show how nearly we can approach the early Florentines even now--when nobody is looking at us.
alpsn10{_}1475{_}I do not know who Mr. Pollard is.
alpsn10{_}1476{_}I never heard of him till I came across a cheap lithograph of his Funeral of Tom Moody in the parlour of a village inn.
alpsn10{_}1477{_}I should not think he ever was an R.A., but he has approached as nearly as the difference between the geniuses of the two countries will allow, to the spirit of the painters who painted in the Campo Santo at Pisa.
alpsn10{_}1478{_}Look, again, at Garrard, at the close of the last century.
alpsn10{_}1479{_}We generally succeed with sporting or quasi-sporting subjects, and our cheap coloured coaching and hunting subjects are almost always good, and often very good indeed.
alpsn10{_}1480{_}We like these things:  therefore we observe them; therefore we soon become able to express them.
alpsn10{_}1481{_}Historical and costume pictures we have no genuine love for; we do not, therefore, go beyond repeating commonplaces concerning them.
alpsn10{_}1482{_}I must reserve other remarks upon this subject for another occasion.
alpsn10{_}1483{_}CHAPTER XIII--Viu, Fucine, and S. Ignazio    I must now return to my young friend at Groscavallo.
alpsn10{_}1484{_}I have published his drawings without his permission, having unfortunately lost his name and address, and being unable therefore to apply to him.
alpsn10{_}1485{_}I hope that, should they ever meet his eye, he will accept this apology and the assurance of my most profound consideration.
alpsn10{_}1486{_}Delighted as I had been with his proposed illustrations, I thought I had better hear some of the letterpress, so I begged him to read me his MS.  My time was short, and he began at once.
alpsn10{_}1487{_}The few introductory pages were very nice, but there was nothing particularly noticeable about them; when, however, he came to his description of the place where we now were, he spoke of a beautiful young lady as attracting his attention on the evening of his arrival.
alpsn10{_}1488{_}It seemed that she was as much struck with him as he with her, and I thought we were going to have a romance, when he proceeded as follows:  "We perceived that we were sympathetic, and in less than a quarter of an hour had exchanged the most solemn vows that we would never marry one another."
alpsn10{_}1489{_}"What?"
alpsn10{_}1490{_}said I, hardly able to believe my ears, "will you kindly read those last words over again?"
alpsn10{_}1491{_}He did so, slowly and distinctly; I caught them beyond all power of mistake, and they were as I have given them above:- "We perceived that we were sympathetic, and in less than a quarter of an hour had exchanged the most solemn vows that we would never marry one another."
alpsn10{_}1492{_}While I was rubbing my eyes and making up my mind whether I had stumbled upon a great satirist or no, I heard a voice from below--"Signor Butler, Signor Butler, la vettura e pronta."
alpsn10{_}1493{_}I had therefore to leave my doubt unsolved, but all the time as we drove down the valley I had the words above quoted ringing in my head.
alpsn10{_}1494{_}If ever any of my readers come across the book itself--for I should hope it will be published--I should be very grateful to them if they will direct my attention to it.
alpsn10{_}1495{_}Another day I went to Ceres, and returned on foot via S. Ignazio.
alpsn10{_}1496{_}S. Ignazio is a famous sanctuary on the very top of a mountain, like that of Sammichele; but it is late, the St. Ignatius being St. Ignatius Loyola, and not the apostolic father.
alpsn10{_}1497{_}I got my dinner at a village inn at the foot of the mountain, and from the window caught sight of a fresco upon the wall of a chapel a few yards off.
alpsn10{_}1498{_}There was a companion to it hardly less interesting, but I had not time to sketch it.
alpsn10{_}1499{_}I do not know what the one I give is intended to represent.
alpsn10{_}1500{_}St. Ignatius is upon a rock, and is pleased with something, but there is nothing to show what it is, except his attitude, which seems to say, "Senza far fatica,"--"You see I can do it quite easily," or, "There is no deception."
alpsn10{_}1501{_}Nor do we easily gather what it is that the Roman centurion is saying to St. Ignatius.
alpsn10{_}1502{_}I cannot make up my mind whether he is merely warning him to beware of the reaction, or whether he is a little scandalised.
alpsn10{_}1503{_}From this village I went up the mountain to the sanctuary of S. Ignazio itself, which looks well from the distance, and commands a striking view, but contains nothing of interest, except a few nice votive pictures.
alpsn10{_}1504{_}From Lanzo I went to Viu, a summer resort largely frequented by the Turinese, but rarely visited by English people.
alpsn10{_}1505{_}There is a good inn at Viu--the one close to where the public conveyance stops--and the neighbourhood is enchanting.
alpsn10{_}1506{_}The little village on the crest of the hill in the distance, to the left of the church, as shown on the preceding page, is called the Colma di S. Giovanni, and is well worth a visit.
alpsn10{_}1507{_}In spring, before the grass is cut, the pastures must be even better than when I saw them in August, and they were then still of almost incredible beauty.
alpsn10{_}1508{_}I went to S. Giovanni by the directest way--descending, that is, to the level of the Stura, crossing it, and then going straight up the mountain.
alpsn10{_}1509{_}I returned by a slight detour so as to take the village of Fucine, a frazione of Viu a little higher up the river.
alpsn10{_}1510{_}I found many picturesque bits; among them the one which I give on the next page.
alpsn10{_}1511{_}It was a grand festa; first they had had mass, then there had been the funzioni, which I never quite understand, and thenceforth till sundown there was a public ball on the bowling ground of a little inn on the Viu side of the bridge.
alpsn10{_}1512{_}The principal inn is on the other side.
alpsn10{_}1513{_}It was here I went and ordered dinner.
alpsn10{_}1514{_}The landlady brought me a minestra, or hodge-podge soup, full of savoury vegetables, and very good; a nice cutlet fried in bread-crumbs, bread and butter ad libitum, and half a bottle of excellent wine.
alpsn10{_}1515{_}She brought all together on a tray, and put them down on the table.
alpsn10{_}1516{_}"It'll come to a franc," said she, "in all, but please to pay first."
alpsn10{_}1517{_}I did so, of course, and she was satisfied.
alpsn10{_}1518{_}A day or two afterwards I went to the same inn, hoping to dine as well and cheaply as before; but I think they must have discovered that I was a forestiere inglese in the meantime, for they did not make me pay first, and charged me normal prices.
alpsn10{_}1519{_}What pretty words they have!
alpsn10{_}1520{_}While eating my dinner I wanted a small plate and asked for it.
alpsn10{_}1521{_}The landlady changed the word I had used, and told a girl to bring me a tondino.
alpsn10{_}1522{_}A tondino is an abbreviation of rotondino, a "little round thing."
alpsn10{_}1523{_}A plate is a tondo, a small plate a tondino.
alpsn10{_}1524{_}The delicacy of expression which their diminutives and intensitives give is untranslateable.
alpsn10{_}1525{_}One day I was asking after a waiter whom I had known in previous years, but who was ill.
alpsn10{_}1526{_}I said I hoped he was not badly off.
alpsn10{_}1527{_}"Oh dear, no," was the answer; "he has a discreta posizionina"--"a snug little sum put by."
alpsn10{_}1528{_}"Is the road to such and such a place difficult?"
alpsn10{_}1529{_}I once inquired.
alpsn10{_}1530{_}"Un tantino," was the answer.
alpsn10{_}1531{_}"Ever such a very little," I suppose, is as near as we can get to this.
alpsn10{_}1532{_}At one inn I asked whether I could have my linen back from the wash by a certain time, and was told it was impossibilissimo.
alpsn10{_}1533{_}I have an Italian friend long resident in England who often introduces English words when talking with me in Italian.
alpsn10{_}1534{_}Thus I have heard him say that such and such a thing is tanto cheapissimo.
alpsn10{_}1535{_}As for their gestures, they are inimitable.
alpsn10{_}1536{_}To say nothing of the pretty little way in which they say "no," by moving the forefinger backwards and forwards once or twice, they have a hundred movements to save themselves the trouble of speaking, which say what they have to say better than any words can do.
alpsn10{_}1537{_}It is delightful to see an Italian move his hand in such way as to show you that you have got to go round a corner.
alpsn10{_}1538{_}Gesture is easier both to make and to understand than speech is.
alpsn10{_}1539{_}Speech is a late acquisition, and in critical moments is commonly discarded in favour of gesture, which is older and more habitual.
alpsn10{_}1540{_}I once saw an Italian explaining something to another and tapping his nose a great deal.
alpsn10{_}1541{_}He became more and more confidential, and the more confidential he became, the more he tapped, till his finger seemed to become glued to, and almost grow into his nose.
alpsn10{_}1542{_}At last the supreme moment came.
alpsn10{_}1543{_}He drew the finger down, pressing it closely against his lower lip, so as to drag it all down and show his gums and the roots of his teeth.
alpsn10{_}1544{_}"There," he seemed to say, "you now know all:  consider me as turned inside out:  my mucous membrane is before you."
alpsn10{_}1545{_}At Fucine, and indeed in all the valleys hereabout, spinning-wheels are not uncommon.
alpsn10{_}1546{_}I also saw a woman sitting in her room with the door opening on to the street, weaving linen at a hand-loom.
alpsn10{_}1547{_}The woman and the hand-loom were both very old and rickety.
alpsn10{_}1548{_}The first and the last specimens of anything, whether animal or vegetable organism, or machine, or institution, are seldom quite satisfactory.
alpsn10{_}1549{_}Some five or six years ago I saw an old gentleman sitting outside the St. Lawrence Hall at Montreal, in Canada, and wearing a pigtail, but it was not a good pigtail; and when the Scotch baron killed the last wolf in Scotland, it was probably a weak, mangy old thing, capable of little further mischief.
alpsn10{_}1550{_}Presently I walked a mile or two up the river, and met a godfather coming along with a cradle on his shoulder; he was followed by two women, one carrying some long wax candles, and the other something wrapped up in a piece of brown paper; they were going to get the child christened at Fucine.
alpsn10{_}1551{_}Soon after I met a priest, and bowed, as a matter of course.
alpsn10{_}1552{_}In towns or places where many foreigners come and go this is unnecessary, but in small out-of-the-way places one should take one's hat off to the priest.
alpsn10{_}1553{_}I mention this because many Englishmen do not know that it is expected of them, and neglect the accustomed courtesy through ignorance.
alpsn10{_}1554{_}Surely, even here in England, if one is in a small country village, off one's beat, and meets the clergyman, it is more polite than not to take off one's hat.
alpsn10{_}1555{_}Viu is one of the places from which pilgrims ascend the Rocca Melone at the beginning of August.
alpsn10{_}1556{_}This is one of the most popular and remarkable pilgrimages of North Italy; the Rocca Melone is 11,000 feet high, and forms a peak so sharp, that there is room for little else than the small wooden chapel which stands at the top of it.
alpsn10{_}1557{_}There is no accommodation whatever, except at some rough barracks (so I have been told) some thousands of feet below the summit.
alpsn10{_}1558{_}These, I was informed, are sometimes so crowded that the people doze standing, and the cold at night is intense, unless under the shelter just referred to; yet some five or six thousand pilgrims ascend on the day and night of the festa--chiefly from Susa, but also from all parts of the valleys of the Dora and the Stura.
alpsn10{_}1559{_}They leave Susa early in the morning, camp out or get shelter in the barracks that evening, reaching the chapel at the top of the Rocca Melone next day.
alpsn10{_}1560{_}I have not made the ascent myself, but it would probably be worth making by one who did not mind the fatigue.
alpsn10{_}1561{_}I may mention that thatch is not uncommon in the Stura valley.
alpsn10{_}1562{_}In the Val Mastallone, and more especially between Civiasco (above Varallo) and Orta, thatch is more common still, and the thatching is often very beautifully done.
alpsn10{_}1563{_}Thatch in a stone country is an indication of German, or at any rate Cisalpine descent, and is among the many proofs of the extent to which German races crossed the Alps and spread far down over Piedmont and Lombardy.
alpsn10{_}1564{_}I was more struck with traces of German influence on the path from Pella on the Lago d'Orta, to the Colma on the way to Varallo, than perhaps anywhere else.
alpsn10{_}1565{_}The churches have a tendency to have pure spires--a thing never seen in Italy proper; clipped yews and box- trees are common; there are lime-trees in the churchyards, and thatch is the rule, not the exception.
alpsn10{_}1566{_}At Rimella in the Val Mastallone, not far off, German is still the current language.
alpsn10{_}1567{_}As I sat sketching, a woman came up to me, and said, "Was machen sic?"
alpsn10{_}1568{_}as a matter of course.
alpsn10{_}1569{_}Rimella is the highest village in its valley, yet if one crosses the saddle at the head of the valley, one does not descend upon a German-speaking district; one descends on the Val Anzasca, where Italian is universally spoken.
alpsn10{_}1570{_}Until recently German was the language of many other villages at the heads of valleys, even though these valleys were themselves entirely surrounded by Italian-speaking people.
alpsn10{_}1571{_}At Alagna in the Val Sesia, German is still spoken.
alpsn10{_}1572{_}Whatever their origin, however, the people are now thoroughly Italianised.
alpsn10{_}1573{_}Nevertheless, as I have already said, it is strange what a number of people one meets among them, whom most people would unhesitatingly pronounce to be English if asked to name their nationality.
alpsn10{_}1574{_}CHAPTER XIV--Sanctuary of Oropa    From Lanzo I went back to Turin, where Jones again joined me, and we resolved to go and see the famous sanctuary of Oropa near Biella.
alpsn10{_}1575{_}Biella is about three hours' railway journey from Turin.
alpsn10{_}1576{_}It is reached by a branch line of some twenty miles, that leaves the main line between Turin and Milan at Santhia.
alpsn10{_}1577{_}Except the view of the Alps, which in clear weather cannot be surpassed, there is nothing of very particular interest between Turin and Santhia, nor need Santhia detain the traveller longer than he can help.
alpsn10{_}1578{_}Biella we found to consist of an upper and a lower town--the upper, as may be supposed, being the older.
alpsn10{_}1579{_}It is at the very junction of the plain and the mountains, and is a thriving place, with more of the busy air of an English commercial town than perhaps any other of its size in North Italy.
alpsn10{_}1580{_}Even in the old town large rambling old palazzi have been converted into factories, and the click of the shuttle is heard in unexpected places.
alpsn10{_}1581{_}We were unable to find that Biella contains any remarkable pictures or other works of art, though they are doubtless to be found by those who have the time to look for them.
alpsn10{_}1582{_}There is a very fine campanile near the post-office, and an old brick baptistery, also hard by; but the church to which both campanile and baptistery belonged, has, as the author of "Round about London" so well says, been "utterly restored;" it cannot be uglier than what we sometimes do, but it is quite as ugly.
alpsn10{_}1583{_}We found an Italian opera company in Biella; peeping through a grating, as many others were doing, we watched the company rehearsing "La forza del destino," which was to be given later in the week.
alpsn10{_}1584{_}The morning after our arrival, we took the daily diligence for Oropa, leaving Biella at eight o'clock.
alpsn10{_}1585{_}Before we were clear of the town we could see the long line of the hospice, and the chapels dotted about near it, high up in a valley at some distance off; presently we were shown another fine building some eight or nine miles away, which we were told was the sanctuary of Graglia.
alpsn10{_}1586{_}About this time the pictures and statuettes of the Madonna began to change their hue and to become black--for the sacred image of Oropa being black, all the Madonnas in her immediate neighbourhood are of the same complexion.
alpsn10{_}1587{_}Underneath some of them is written, "Nigra sum sed sum formosa," which, as a rule, was more true as regards the first epithet than the second.
alpsn10{_}1588{_}It was not market-day, but streams of people were coming to the town.
alpsn10{_}1589{_}Many of them were pilgrims returning from the sanctuary, but more were bringing the produce of their farms, or the work of their hands for sale.
alpsn10{_}1590{_}We had to face a steady stream of chairs, which were coming to town in baskets upon women's heads.
alpsn10{_}1591{_}Each basket contained twelve chairs, though whether it is correct to say that the basket contained the chairs--when the chairs were all, so to say, froth running over the top of the basket--is a point I cannot settle.
alpsn10{_}1592{_}Certainly we had never seen anything like so many chairs before, and felt almost as though we had surprised nature in the laboratory wherefrom she turns out the chair supply of the world.
alpsn10{_}1593{_}The road continued through a succession of villages almost running into one another for a long way after Biella was passed, but everywhere we noticed the same air of busy thriving industry which we had seen in Biella itself.
alpsn10{_}1594{_}We noted also that a preponderance of the people had light hair, while that of the children was frequently nearly white, as though the infusion of German blood was here stronger even than usual.
alpsn10{_}1595{_}Though so thickly peopled, the country was of great beauty.
alpsn10{_}1596{_}Near at hand were the most exquisite pastures close shaven after their second mowing, gay with autumnal crocuses, and shaded with stately chestnuts; beyond were rugged mountains, in a combe on one of which we saw Oropa itself now gradually nearing; behind and below, many villages with vineyards and terraces cultivated to the highest perfection; further on, Biella already distant, and beyond this a "big stare," as an American might say, over the plains of Lombardy from Turin to Milan, with the Apennines from Genoa to Bologna hemming the horizon.
alpsn10{_}1597{_}On the road immediate before us, we still faced the same steady stream of chairs flowing ever Biella-ward.
alpsn10{_}1598{_}After a couple of hours the houses became more rare; we got above the sources of the chair-stream; bits of rough rock began to jut out from the pasture; here and there the rhododendron began to show itself by the roadside; the chestnuts left off along a line as level as though cut with a knife; stone-roofed cascine began to abound, with goats and cattle feeding near them; the booths of the religious trinket-mongers increased; the blind, halt, and maimed became more importunate, and the foot-passengers were more entirely composed of those whose object was, or had been, a visit to the sanctuary itself.
alpsn10{_}1599{_}The numbers of these pilgrims--generally in their Sunday's best, and often comprising the greater part of a family--were so great, though there was no special festa, as to testify to the popularity of the institution.
alpsn10{_}1600{_}They generally walked barefoot, and carried their shoes and stockings; their baggage consisted of a few spare clothes, a little food, and a pot or pan or two to cook with.
alpsn10{_}1601{_}Many of them looked very tired, and had evidently tramped from long distances--indeed, we saw costumes belonging to valleys which could not be less than two or three days distant.
alpsn10{_}1602{_}They were almost invariably quiet, respectable, and decently clad, sometimes a little merry, but never noisy, and none of them tipsy.
alpsn10{_}1603{_}As we travelled along the road, we must have fallen in with several hundreds of these pilgrims coming and going; nor is this likely to be an extravagant estimate, seeing that the hospice can make up more than five thousand beds.
alpsn10{_}1604{_}By eleven we were at the sanctuary itself.
alpsn10{_}1605{_}Fancy a quiet upland valley, the floor of which is about the same height as the top of Snowdon, shut in by lofty mountains upon three sides, while on the fourth the eye wanders at will over the plains below.
alpsn10{_}1606{_}Fancy finding a level space in such a valley watered by a beautiful mountain stream, and nearly filled by a pile of collegiate buildings, not less important than those, we will say, of Trinity College, Cambridge.
