alpsn10{_}2592{_}I do not know wherein its peculiar charm lies, but it is the best of all the villages of a kindred character that I know.
alpsn10{_}2593{_}Below is a sketch of it as it appears from the cemetery.
alpsn10{_}2594{_}There is another good view from behind the village; at sunset this second view becomes remarkably fine.
alpsn10{_}2595{_}The houses are in deep cool shadow, but the mountains behind take the evening sun, and are sometimes of an incredible splendour.
alpsn10{_}2596{_}It is fine to watch the shadows creeping up them, and the colour that remains growing richer and richer until the whole is extinguished; this view, however, I am unable to give.
alpsn10{_}2597{_}I hold Signor Dazio of Fusio so much as one of my most particular and valued friends, and I have such special affection for Fusio itself, that the reader must bear in mind that he is reading an account given by a partial witness.
alpsn10{_}2598{_}Nevertheless, all private preferences apart, I think he will find Fusio a hard place to beat.
alpsn10{_}2599{_}At the end of June and in July the flowers are at their best, and they are more varied and beautiful than anywhere else I know.
alpsn10{_}2600{_}At the very end of July and the beginning of August the people cut their hay, and then for a while the glory of the place is gone, but by the end of August or the beginning of September the grass has grown long enough to re-cover the slopes with a velvety verdure, and though the flowers are shorn, yet so they are from other places also.
alpsn10{_}2601{_}There are many walks in the neighbourhood for those who do not mind mountain paths.
alpsn10{_}2602{_}The most beautiful of them all is to the valley of Sambucco, the upper end which is not more than half-an-hour from Signor Dazio's hotel.
alpsn10{_}2603{_}For some time one keeps to the path through the wooded gorge, and with the river foaming far below; in early morning while this path is in shade, or, again, after sunset, it is one of the most beautiful of its kind that I know.
alpsn10{_}2604{_}After a while a gate is reached, and an open upland valley is entered upon-- evidently an old lake filled up, and neither very broad nor very long, but grassed all over, and with the river winding through it like an English brook.
alpsn10{_}2605{_}This is the valley of Sambucco.
alpsn10{_}2606{_}There are two collections of stalle for the cattle, or monti--one at the nearer end and the other at the farther.
alpsn10{_}2607{_}The floor of the valley can hardly be less than 5000 feet above the sea.
alpsn10{_}2608{_}I shall never forget the pleasure with which I first came upon it.
alpsn10{_}2609{_}I had long wanted an ideal upland valley; as a general rule high valleys are too narrow, and have little or no level ground.
alpsn10{_}2610{_}If they have any at all there often is too much as with the one where Andermatt and Hospenthal are--which would in some respects do very well--and too much cultivated, and do not show their height.
alpsn10{_}2611{_}An upland valley should first of all be in an Italian-speaking country; then it should have a smooth, grassy, perfectly level floor of say neither much more nor less than a hundred and fifty yards in breadth and half-a-mile in length.
alpsn10{_}2612{_}A small river should go babbling through it with occasional smooth parts, so as to take the reflections of the surrounding mountains.
alpsn10{_}2613{_}It should have three or four fine larches or pines scattered about it here and there, but not more.
alpsn10{_}2614{_}It should be completely land- locked, and there should be nothing in the way of human handiwork save a few chalets, or a small chapel and a bridge, but no tilled land whatever.
alpsn10{_}2615{_}Here oven in summer the evening air will be crisp, and the dew will form as soon as the sun goes off; but the mountains at one end of it will keep the last rays of the sun.
alpsn10{_}2616{_}It is then the valley is at its best, especially if the goats and cattle are coming together to be milked.
alpsn10{_}2617{_}The valley of Sambucco has all this and a great deal more, to say nothing of the fact that there are excellent trout in it.
alpsn10{_}2618{_}I have shown it to friends at different times, and they have all agreed with me that for a valley neither too high nor too low, nor too big nor too little, the valley of Sambucco is one of the best that any of us know of--I mean to look at and enjoy, for I suppose as regards painting it is hopeless.
alpsn10{_}2619{_}I think it can be well rendered by the following piece of music as by anything else:- {33}  [At this point in the book a music score is given]  One day Signor Dazio brought us in a chamois foot.
alpsn10{_}2620{_}He explained to us that chamois were now in season, but that even when they were not, they were sometimes to be had, inasmuch as they occasionally fell from the rocks and got killed.
alpsn10{_}2621{_}As we looked at it we could not help reflecting that, wonderful as the provisions of animal and vegetable organisms often are, the marvels of adaptation are sometimes almost exceeded by the feats which an animal will perform with a very simple and even clumsy instrument if it knows how to use it.
alpsn10{_}2622{_}A chamois foot is a smooth and slippery thing, such as no respectable bootmaker would dream of offering to a mountaineer: there is not a nail in it, nor even an apology for a nail; the surefootedness of its owner is an assumption only--a piece of faith or impudence which fulfils itself.
alpsn10{_}2623{_}If some other animal were to induce the chamois to believe that it should at the least have feet with suckers to them, like a fly, before venturing in such breakneck places, or if by any means it could get to know how bad a foot it really has, there would soon be no more chamois.
alpsn10{_}2624{_}The chamois continues to exist through its absolute refusal to hear reason upon the matter.
alpsn10{_}2625{_}But the whole question is one of extreme intricacy; all we know is that some animals and plants, like some men, devote great pains to the perfection of the mechanism with which they wish to work, while others rather scorn appliances, and concentrate their attention upon the skilful use of whatever they happen to have.
alpsn10{_}2626{_}I think, however, that in the clumsiness of the chamois foot must lie the explanation of the fact that sometimes when chamois are out of season, they do nevertheless actually tumble off the rocks and get killed; being killed, of course it is only natural that they should sometimes be found, and if found, be eaten; but they are not good for much.
alpsn10{_}2627{_}After a day or two's stay in this delightful place, we left at six o'clock one brilliant morning in September for Dalpe and Faido, accompanied by the excellent Signor Guglielmoni as guide.
alpsn10{_}2628{_}There are two main passes from Fusio into the Val Leventina--the one by the Sassello Grande to Nante and Airolo, and the other by the Alpe di Campolungo to Dalpe.
alpsn10{_}2629{_}Neither should be attempted by strangers without a guide, though neither of them presents the smallest difficulty.
alpsn10{_}2630{_}There is a third and longer pass by the Lago di Naret to Bedretto, but I have never been over this.
alpsn10{_}2631{_}The other two are both good; on the whole, however, I think I prefer the second.
alpsn10{_}2632{_}Signor Guglielmoni led us over the freshest grassy slopes conceivable--slopes that four or five weeks earlier had been gay with tiger and Turk's-cap lilies, and the flaunting arnica, and every flower that likes mountain company.
alpsn10{_}2633{_}After a three hours' walk we reached the top of the pass, from whence on the one hand one can see the Basodino glacier, and on the other the great Rheinwald glaciers above Olivone.
alpsn10{_}2634{_}Other small glaciers show in valleys near Biasca which I know nothing about, and which I imagine to be almost a terra incognita, except to the inhabitants of such villages as Malvaglia in the Val Blenio.
alpsn10{_}2635{_}When near the top of the pass we heard the whistle of a marmot.
alpsn10{_}2636{_}Guglielmoni told us he had a tame one once which was very fond of him.
alpsn10{_}2637{_}It slept all the winter, but turned round once a fortnight to avoid lying too long upon one side.
alpsn10{_}2638{_}When it woke up from its winter sleep it no longer recognised him, but bit him savagely right through the finger; by and by its recollection returned to it, and it apologised.
alpsn10{_}2639{_}From the summit, which is about 7600 feet above the sea, the path descends over the roughest ground that is to be found on the whole route.
alpsn10{_}2640{_}Here there are good specimens of asbestos to be picked up abundantly, and the rocks are full of garnets; after about six or seven hundred feet the Alpe di Campolungo is reached, and this again is an especially favourite place with me.
alpsn10{_}2641{_}It is an old lake filled up, surrounded by peaks and precipices where some snow rests all the year round, and traversed by a stream.
alpsn10{_}2642{_}Here, just as we had done lunching, we were joined by a family of knife-grinders, who were also crossing from the Val Maggia to the Val Leventina.
alpsn10{_}2643{_}We had eaten all we had with us except our bread; this Guglielmoni gave to one of the boys, who seemed as much pleased with it as if it had been cake.
alpsn10{_}2644{_}Then after taking a look at the Lago di Tremorgio, a beautiful lake some hundreds of feet below, we went on to the Alpe di Cadonighino where our guide left us.
alpsn10{_}2645{_}At this point pines begin, and soon the path enters them; after a while we catch sight of Prato, and eventually come down upon Dalpe.
alpsn10{_}2646{_}In another hour and a quarter Faido is reached.
alpsn10{_}2647{_}The descent to Faido from the summit of the pass is much greater than the ascent from Fusio, for Faido is not more than 2300 feet above the sea, whereas, as I have said, Fusio is over 4200 feet.
alpsn10{_}2648{_}The descent from the top of the pass to Faido is about 5300 feet, while to Fusio it is only 3400.
alpsn10{_}2649{_}The reader, therefore, will see that he had better go from Fusio to Faido, and not vice versa, unless he is a good walker.
alpsn10{_}2650{_}From Faido we returned home.
alpsn10{_}2651{_}We looked at nothing between the top of the St. Gothard Pass and Boulogne, nor did we again begin to take any interest in life till we saw the science-ridden, art- ridden, culture-ridden, afternoon-tea-ridden cliffs of Old England rise upon the horizon.
alpsn10{_}2652{_}APPENDIX A--Wednesbury Cocking  (See p. 55)    I know nothing of the date of this remarkable ballad, or the source from which it comes.
alpsn10{_}2653{_}I have heard one who should know say, that when he was a boy at Shrewsbury school it was done into Greek hexameters, the lines (with a various reading in them):   "The colliers and nailers left work, And all to old Scroggins' went jogging;"   being translated:   [Greek text]   I have been at some pains to find out more about this translation, but have failed to do so.
alpsn10{_}2654{_}The ballad itself is as follows:   At Wednesbury there was a cocking, A match between Newton and Scroggins; The colliers and nailers left work, And all to old Spittle's went jogging.
alpsn10{_}2655{_}To see this noble sport, Many noblemen resorted; And though they'd but little money, Yet that little they freely sported.
alpsn10{_}2656{_}There was Jeffery and Colborn from Hampton, And Dusty from Bilston was there; Flummery he came from Darlaston, And he was as rude as a bear.
alpsn10{_}2657{_}There was old Will from Walsall, And Smacker from Westbromwich come; Blind Robin he came from Rowley, And staggering he went home.
alpsn10{_}2658{_}Ralph Moody came hobbling along, As though he some cripple was mocking, To join in the blackguard throng, That met at Wednesbury cocking.
alpsn10{_}2659{_}He borrowed a trifle of Doll, To back old Taverner's grey; He laid fourpence-halfpenny to fourpence, He lost and went broken away.
alpsn10{_}2660{_}But soon he returned to the pit, For he'd borrowed a trifle more money, And ventured another large bet, Along with blobbermouth Coney.
alpsn10{_}2661{_}When Coney demanded his money, As is usual on all such occasions, He cried, -- thee, if thee don't hold thy rattle, I'll pay thee as Paul paid the Ephasians.
alpsn10{_}2662{_}The morning's sport being over, Old Spittle a dinner proclaimed, Each man he should dine for a groat, If he grumbled he ought to be --, For there was plenty of beef, But Spittle he swore by his troth, That never a man should dine Till he ate his noggin of broth.
alpsn10{_}2663{_}The beef it was old and tough, Off a bull that was baited to death, Barney Hyde got a lump in his throat, That had like to have stopped his breath, The company all fell into confusion, At seeing poor Barney Hyde choke; So they took him into the kitchen, And held him over the smoke.
alpsn10{_}2664{_}They held him so close to the fire, He frizzled just like a beef-steak, They then threw him down on the floor, Which had like to have broken his neck.
alpsn10{_}2665{_}One gave him a kick on the stomach, Another a kick on the brow, His wife said, Throw him into the stable, And he'll be better just now.
alpsn10{_}2666{_}Then they all returned to the pit, And the fighting went forward again; Six battles were fought on each side, And the next was to decide the main.
alpsn10{_}2667{_}For they were two famous cocks As ever this country bred, Scroggins's a dark-winged black, And Newton's a shift-winged red.
alpsn10{_}2668{_}The conflict was hard on both sides, Till Brassy's black-winged was choked; The colliers were tarnationly vexed, And the nailers were sorely provoked.
alpsn10{_}2669{_}Peter Stevens he swore a great oath, That Scroggins had played his cock foul; Scroggins gave him a kick on the head, And cried, Yea,--thy soul.
alpsn10{_}2670{_}The company then fell in discord, A bold, bold fight did ensue; -, -, and bite was the word, Till the Walsall men all were subdued.
alpsn10{_}2671{_}Ralph Moody bit off a man's nose, And wished that he could have him slain, So they trampled both cocks to death, And they made a draw of the main.
alpsn10{_}2672{_}The cock-pit was near to the church, An ornament unto the town; On one side an old coal pit, The other well gorsed around.
alpsn10{_}2673{_}Peter Hadley peeped through the gorse, In order to see them fight; Spittle jobbed out his eye with a fork, And said, -- thee, it served thee right.
alpsn10{_}2674{_}Some people may think this strange, Who Wednesbury never knew; But those who have ever been there, Will not have the least doubt it's true; For they are as savage by nature, And guilty of deeds the most shocking; Jack Baker whacked his own father, And thus ended Wednesbury cocking.
alpsn10{_}2675{_}APPENDIX B--Reforms Instituted at S. Michele in the year 1478  (See p. 105)    The palmiest days of the sanctuary were during the time that Rodolfo di Montebello or Mombello was abbot--that is to say, roughly, between the years 1325-60.
alpsn10{_}2676{_}"His rectorate," says Claretta, "was the golden age of the Abbey of La Chiusa, which reaped the glory acquired by its head in the difficult negotiations entrusted to him by his princes.
alpsn10{_}2677{_}But after his death, either lot or intrigue caused the election to fall upon those who prepared the ruin of one of the most ancient and illustrious monasteries in Piedmont."
alpsn10{_}2678{_}{34}  By the last quarter of the fifteenth century things got so bad that a commission of inquiry was held under one Giovanni di Varax in the year 1478.
alpsn10{_}2679{_}The following extracts from the ordinances then made may not be unwelcome to the reader.
alpsn10{_}2680{_}The document from which they are taken is to be found, pp.
alpsn10{_}2681{_}322-336 of Claretta's work.
alpsn10{_}2682{_}The text is evidently in many places corrupt or misprinted, and there are several words which I have looked for in vain in all the dictionaries--Latin, Italian, and French--in the reading-room of the British Museum which seemed in the least likely to contain them.
alpsn10{_}2683{_}I should say that for this translation, I have availed myself, in part, of the assistance of a well-known mediaeval scholar, the Rev.
alpsn10{_}2684{_}Ponsonby A. Lyons, but he is in no way responsible for the translation as a whole.
alpsn10{_}2685{_}After a preamble, stating the names of the commissioners, with the objects of the commission and the circumstances under which it had been called together, the following orders were unanimously agreed upon, to wit:-  "Firstly, That repairs urgently required to prevent the building from falling into a ruinous state (as shown by the ocular testimony of the commissioners, assisted by competent advisers whom they instructed to survey the fabric), be paid for by a true tithe, to be rendered by all priors, provosts, and agents directly subject to the monastery.
alpsn10{_}2686{_}This tithe is to be placed in the hands of two merchants to be chosen by the bishop commendatory, and a sum is to be taken from it for the restoration of the fountain which played formerly in the monastery.
alpsn10{_}2687{_}The proctors who collect the tithes are to be instructed by the abbot and commendatory not to press harshly upon the contributories by way of expense and labour; and the money when collected is, as already said, to be placed in the hands of two suitable merchants, clients of the said monastery, who shall hold it on trust to pay it for the above-named purposes, as the reverends the commendatory and chamberlain and treasurer of the said monastery shall direct.
alpsn10{_}2688{_}In the absence of one of these three the order of the other two shall be sufficient.
alpsn10{_}2689{_}"Item, it is ordered that the mandes, {35} or customary alms, be made daily to the value of what would suffice for the support of four monks.
alpsn10{_}2690{_}"Item, that the offices in the gift of the monastery be conferred by the said reverend the lord commendatory, and that those which have been hitherto at the personal disposition of the abbot be reserved for the pleasure of the Apostolic See.
alpsn10{_}2691{_}Item, that no one do beg a benefice without reasonable cause and consonancy of justice.
alpsn10{_}2692{_}Item, that those who have had books, privileges, or other documents belonging to the monastery do restore them to the treasury within three months from the publication of these presents, under pain of excommunication.
alpsn10{_}2693{_}Item, that no one henceforth take privileges or other documents from the monastery without a deposit of caution money, or taking oath to return the same within three months, under like pain of excommunication.
alpsn10{_}2694{_}Item, that no laymen do enter the treasury of the monastery without the consent of the prior of cloister, {36} nor without the presence of those who hold the keys of the treasury, or of three monks, and that those who hold the keys do not deliver them to laymen.
alpsn10{_}2695{_}Item, it is ordered that the places subject to the said monastery be visited every five years by persons in holy orders, and by seculars; and that, in like manner, every five years a general chapter be held, but this period may be extended or shortened for reasonable cause, and the proctors-general are to be bound in each chapter to bring their procurations, and at some chapter each monk is to bring the account of the fines and all other rights appertaining to his benefice, drawn up by a notary in public form, and undersigned by him, that they may be kept in the treasury, and this under pain of suspension.
alpsn10{_}2696{_}Item, that henceforth neither the office of prior nor any other benefice be conferred upon laymen.
alpsn10{_}2697{_}The lord abbot is in future to be charged with the expense of all new buildings that are erected within the precincts of the monastery.
alpsn10{_}2698{_}He is also to give four pittances or suppers to the convent during infirmary time, and six pints of wine according to the custom.
alpsn10{_}2699{_}{37}  Furthermore, he is to keep beds in the monastery for the use of guests, and other monks shall return these beds to the chamberlain on the departure of the guests, and it shall be the chamberlain's business to attend to this matter.
alpsn10{_}2700{_}Item, delinquent monks are to be punished within the monastery and not without it.
alpsn10{_}2701{_}Item, the monks shall not presume to give an order for more than two days' board at the expense of the monastery, in the inns at S. Ambrogio, during each week, and they shall not give orders for fifteen days unless they have relations on a journey staying with them, or nobles, or persons above suspicion, and the same be understood as applying to officials and cloistered persons.
alpsn10{_}2702{_}{38}  "Item, within twelve months from date the monks are to be at the expense of building an almshouse in S. Ambrogio, where one or two of the oldest and most respected among them are to reside, and have their portions there, and receive those who are in religion.
alpsn10{_}2703{_}Item, no monk is to wear his hair longer than two fingers broad.
alpsn10{_}2704{_}{39} Item, no hounds are to be kept in the monastery for hunting, nor any dogs save watch-dogs.
alpsn10{_}2705{_}Persons in religion who come to the monastery are to be entertained there for two days, during which time the cellarer is to give them bread and wine, and the pittancer {40} pittance.
alpsn10{_}2706{_}"Item, women of bad character, and indeed all women, are forbidden the monk's apartments without the prior's license, except in times of indulgence, or such as are noble or above suspicion.
alpsn10{_}2707{_}Not even are the women from San Pietro, or any suspected women, to be admitted without the prior's permission.
alpsn10{_}2708{_}"The monks are to be careful how they hold converse with suspected women, and are not to be found in the houses of such persons, or they will be punished.
alpsn10{_}2709{_}Item, the epistle and gospel at high mass are to be said by the monks in church, and in Lent the epistle is to be said by one monk or sub-deacon.
alpsn10{_}2710{_}"Item, two candelabra are to be kept above the altar when mass is being said, and the lord abbot is to provide the necessary candles.
alpsn10{_}2711{_}"Any one absent from morning or evening mass is to be punished by the prior, if his absence arises from negligence.
alpsn10{_}2712{_}"The choir, and the monks residing in the monastery, are to be provided with books and a convenient breviary {41} .
alpsn10{_}2713{_}.
alpsn10{_}2714{_}.
alpsn10{_}2715{_}according to ancient custom and statute, nor can those things be sold which are necessary or useful to the convent.
alpsn10{_}2716{_}* * *  "Item, all the religious who are admitted and enter the monastery and religion, shall bring one alb and one amice, to be delivered into the hands of the treasurer and preserved by him for the use of the church.
alpsn10{_}2717{_}* * *  "The treasurer is to have the books that are in daily use in the choir re-bound, and to see that the capes which are unsewn, and all the ecclesiastical vestments under his care are kept in proper repair.
alpsn10{_}2718{_}He is to have the custody of the plate belonging to the monastery, and to hold a key of the treasury.
alpsn10{_}2719{_}He is to furnish in each year an inventory of the property of which he has charge, and to hand the same over to the lord abbot.
alpsn10{_}2720{_}He is to make one common pittance {42} of bread and wine on the day of the feast of St. Nicholas in December, according to custom; and if it happens to be found necessary to make a chest to hold charters, &c., the person whose business it shall be to make this shall be bound to make it.
alpsn10{_}2721{_}"As regards the office of almoner, the almoner shall each day give alms in the monastery to the faithful poor--to wit, barley bread to the value of twopence current money, and on Holy Thursday he shall make an alms of threepence {43} to all comers, and shall give them a plate of beans and a drink of wine.
alpsn10{_}2722{_}Item, he is to make alms four times a year--that is to say, on Christmas Day, on Quinquagesima Sunday, and at the feasts of Pentecost and Easter; and he is to give to every man a small loaf of barley and a grilled pork chop, {44} the third of a pound in weight.
alpsn10{_}2723{_}Item, he shall make a pittance to the convent on the vigil of St. Martin of bread, wine, and mincemeat dumplings, {45}--that is to say, for each person two loaves and two .
alpsn10{_}2724{_}.
alpsn10{_}2725{_}.
alpsn10{_}2726{_}{46} of wine and some leeks,--and he is to lay out sixty shillings (?)
alpsn10{_}2727{_}in fish and seasoning, and all the servants are to have a ration of dumplings; and in the morning he is to give them a dumpling cooked in oil, and a quarter of a loaf, and some wine.
alpsn10{_}2728{_}Item, he shall give another pittance on the feast of St. James--to wit, a good sheep and some cabbages {47} with seasoning.
alpsn10{_}2729{_}"Item, during infirmary time he must provide four meat suppers and two pints {48} (?)
alpsn10{_}2730{_}of wine, and a pittance of mincemeat dumplings during the rogation days, as do the sacristan and the butler.
alpsn10{_}2731{_}He is also to give each monk one bundle of straw in every year, and to keep a servant who shall bring water from the spring for the service of the mass and for holy water, and light the fire for the barber, and wait at table, and do all else that is reasonable and usual; and the said almoner shall also keep a towel in the church for drying the hands, and he shall make preparation for the mandes on Holy Thursday, both in the monastery and in the cloister.
alpsn10{_}2732{_}Futhermore, he must keep beds in the hospital of S. Ambrogio, and keep the said hospital in such condition that Christ's poor may be received there in orderly and godly fashion; he must also maintain the chapel of St. Nicholas, and keep the chapel of St. James in a state of repair, and another part of the building contiguous to the chapel.
alpsn10{_}2733{_}Item, it shall devolve upon the chamberlain to pay yearly to each of the monks of the said monastery of St. Martin who say mass, except those of them who hold office, the sum of six florins and six groats, {49} and to the treasurer, precentor, and surveyor, {50} to each one of them the same sum for their clothing, and to each of the young monks who do not say mass four florins and six groats.
alpsn10{_}2734{_}And in every year he is to do one O {51} for the greater priorate {52} during Advent.
alpsn10{_}2735{_}Those who have benefices and who are resident within the monastery, but whose benefice does not amount to the value of their clothes, are to receive their clothes according to the existing custom.
alpsn10{_}2736{_}"Item, the pittancer shall give a pittance of cheese and eggs to each of the monks on every day from the feast of Easter to the feast of the Holy Cross in September--to wit, three quarters of a pound of cheese; but when there is a principal processional duplex feast, each monk is to have a pound of cheese per diem, except on fast days, when he is to have half a pound only.
alpsn10{_}2737{_}Also on days when there is a principal or processional feast, each one of them, including the hebdomadary, is to have five eggs.
alpsn10{_}2738{_}Also, from the feast of Easter to the octave of St. John the Baptist the pittancer is to serve out old cheese, and new cheese from the octave of St. John the Baptist to the feast of St. Michael.
alpsn10{_}2739{_}From the feast of St. Michael to Quinquagesima the cheese is to be of medium quality.
alpsn10{_}2740{_}From the least of the Holy Cross in September until Lent the pittancer must serve out to each monk three quarters of a pound of cheese, if it is a feast of twelve lessons, and if it is a feast of three lessons, whether a week-day or a vigil, the pittancer is to give each monk but half a pound of cheese.
alpsn10{_}2741{_}He is also to give all the monks during Advent nine pounds of wax extra allowance, and it is not proper that the pittancer should weigh out cheese for any one on a Friday unless it be a principal processional or duplex feast, or a principal octave.
alpsn10{_}2742{_}It is also proper, seeing there is no fast from the feast of Christmas to the octave of the Epiphany, that every man should have his three quarters of a pound of cheese per diem.
alpsn10{_}2743{_}Also, on Christmas and Easter days the pittancer shall provide five dumplings per monk per diem, and one plate of sausage meat, {53} and he shall also give to each of the servants on the said two days five dumplings for each several day; and the said pittancer on Christmas Day and on the day of St. John the Baptist shall make a relish, {54} or seasoning, and give to each monk one good glass thereof, that is to say, the fourth part of one {55} for each monk--to wit, on the first, second, and third day of the feast of the Nativity, the Circumcision, the Epiphany, and the Purification of the Blessed Virgin; and the pittancer is to put spice in the said relish, and the cellarer is to provide wine and honey, and during infirmary time those who are being bled are to receive no pittance from the pittancer.
alpsn10{_}2744{_}Further, from the feast of Easter to that of the Cross of September, there is no fast except on the prescribed vigils; each monk, therefore, should always have three quarters of a pound of cheese after celebration on a week-day until the above-named day.
alpsn10{_}2745{_}Further, the pittancer is to provide for three mandes in each week during the whole year, excepting Lent, and for each mande he is to find three pounds of cheese.
alpsn10{_}2746{_}From the feast of St. Michael to that of St. Andrew he is to provide for an additional mande in each week.
alpsn10{_}2747{_}Item, he is to pay the prior of the cloister six florins for his fine {56} .
alpsn10{_}2748{_}.
alpsn10{_}2749{_}.
alpsn10{_}2750{_}and three florins to the .
alpsn10{_}2751{_}.
alpsn10{_}2752{_}.
alpsn10{_}2753{_}.
alpsn10{_}2754{_}{57} and he should also give five eggs per diem to the hebdomadary of the high altar, except in Lent.
alpsn10{_}2755{_}Further, he is to give to the woodman, the baker, the keeper of the church, the servants of the Infirmary, the servant at the Eleemosynary, and the stableman, to each of them one florin in every year.
alpsn10{_}2756{_}Item, any monks who leave the monastery before vespers when it is not a fast, shall lose one quarter of a pound of cheese even though they return to the monastery after vespers but if it is a fast day, they are to lose nothing.
alpsn10{_}2757{_}Item, the pittancer is to serve out mashed beans to the servants of the convent during Lent as well as to those who are in religion, and at this season he is to provide the prior of the cloister and the hebdomadary with bruised cicerate; {58} but if any one of the same is hebdomadary, he is only to receive one portion.
alpsn10{_}2758{_}If there are two celebrating high mass at the high altar, each of them is to receive one plate of the said bruised cicerate.
alpsn10{_}2759{_}"As regards the office of cantor, the cantor is to intone the antiphon 'ad benedictus ad magnificat' at terce, {59} and at all other services, and he is himself to intone the antiphons or provide a substitute who can intone them; and he is to intone the psalms according to custom.
alpsn10{_}2760{_}Also if there is any cloistered person who has begun his week of being hebdomadary, and falls into such sickness that he cannot celebrate the same, the cantor is to say or celebrate three masses.
alpsn10{_}2761{_}The cantor is to lead all the monks of the choir at matins, high mass, vespers, and on all other occasions.
alpsn10{_}2762{_}On days when there is a processional duplex feast, he is to write down the order of the office; that is to say, those who are to say the invitatory, {60} the lessons, the epistle of the gospel {61} and those who are to wear copes at high mass and at vespers.
alpsn10{_}2763{_}The cantor must sing the processional hymns which are sung on entering the church, but he is exempt from taking his turn of being hebdomadary by reason of his intoning the offices; and he is to write down the names of those who celebrate low masses and of those who get them said by proxy; and he is to report these last to the prior that they may be punished.
alpsn10{_}2764{_}The cantor or his delegate is to read in the refectory during meal times and during infirmary time, and he who reads in the refectory is to have a quart [?]
alpsn10{_}2765{_}of bread, as also are the two junior monks who wait at table.
alpsn10{_}2766{_}The cantor is to instruct the boys in the singing of the office and in morals, and is to receive their portions of bread, wine and pittance, and besides all this he is to receive one florin for each of them, and he is to keep them decently; and the prior is to certify himself upon this matter, and to see to it that he victuals them properly and gives them their food.
alpsn10{_}2767{_}"The sacristan is to provide all the lights of the church whether oil or wax, and he is to give out small candles to the hebdomadary, and to keep the eight lamps that burn both night and day supplied with oil.
alpsn10{_}2768{_}He is to keep the lamps in repair and to buy new ones if the old are broken, and he is to provide the incense.
alpsn10{_}2769{_}He is to maintain the covered chapel of St. Nicholas, and the whole church except the portico of the same; and the lord abbot is to provide sound timber for doors and other necessaries.
alpsn10{_}2770{_}He is to keep the frames {62} of the bells in repair, and also the ropes for the same, and during Lent he is to provide two pittances of eels to the value of eighteen groats for each pittance, and one other pittance of dumplings and seasoning during rogation time, to wit, five dumplings cooked in oil for each person, and one quart of bread and wine, and all the house domestics and serving men of the convent who may be present are to have the same.
alpsn10{_}2771{_}At this time all the monks are to have one quarter of a pound of cheese from the sacristan.
alpsn10{_}2772{_}And the said sacristan should find the convent two pittances during infirmary time and two pints {63} of wine, and two suppers, one of chicken and salt meat, with white chestnuts, inasmuch as there is only to be just so much chicken as is sufficient.
alpsn10{_}2773{_}Item, he is to keep the church clean.
alpsn10{_}2774{_}Item, he has to pay to the keeper of the church one measure of barley, and eighteen groats for his clothes yearly, and every Martinmas he is to pay to the cantor sixty soldi, and he shall place a {64} .
alpsn10{_}2775{_}.
alpsn10{_}2776{_}.
alpsn10{_}2777{_}or boss {65} in the choir during Lent.
alpsn10{_}2778{_}Also he must do one O in Advent and take charge of all the ornaments of the altars and all the relics.
alpsn10{_}2779{_}Also on high days and when there is a procession he is to keep the paschal candle before the altar, as is customary, but on other days he shall keep a burning lamp only, and when the candle is burning the lamp may be extinguished.
alpsn10{_}2780{_}* * *  "As touching the office of infirmarer, the infirmarer is to keep the whole convent fifteen days during infirmary time, to wit, the one-half of them for fifteen days and the other half for another fifteen days, except that on the first and last days all the monks will be in the infirmary.
alpsn10{_}2781{_}Also when he makes a pittance he is to give the monks beef and mutton, {66} sufficient in quantity and quality, and to receive their portions.
alpsn10{_}2782{_}The prior of the cloister, cantor, and cellarer may be in the infirmary the whole month.
alpsn10{_}2783{_}And the infirmarer is to keep a servant, who shall go and buy meat three times a week, to wit, on Saturdays, Mondays, and Wednesdays, but at the expense of the sender, and the said servant shall on the days following prepare the meat at the expense of the infirmarer; and he shall salt it and make seasoning as is customary, to wit, on all high days and days when there is a processional duplex feast, and on other days.
alpsn10{_}2784{_}On the feast of St. Michael he shall serve out a seasoning made of sage and onions; but the said servant shall not be bound to go and buy meat during Advent, and on Septuagesima and Quinquagesima Sundays he shall serve out seasoning.
alpsn10{_}2785{_}Also when the infirmarer serves out fresh meat, he is to provide fine salt.
alpsn10{_}2786{_}Also the said servant is to go and fetch medicine once or oftener when necessary, at the expense of the sick person, and to visit him.
alpsn10{_}2787{_}If the sick person requires it, he can have aid in the payment of his doctor, and the lord abbot is to pay for the doctor and medicines of all cloistered persons.
alpsn10{_}2788{_}"On the principal octaves the monks are to have seasoning, but during the main feasts they are to have seasoning upon the first day only.
alpsn10{_}2789{_}The infirmarer is not bound to do anything or serve out anything on days when no flesh is eaten.
alpsn10{_}2790{_}The cellarer is to do this, and during the times of the said infirmaries, the servants of the monastery and convent are to be, as above, on the same footing as those who are in religion, that is to say, half of them are to be bled during one fifteen days, and the other half during the other fifteen days, as is customary.
alpsn10{_}2791{_}"Item, touching the office of cellarer, it is ordered that the cellarer do serve out to the whole convent bread, wine, oil, and salt; as much of these two last as any one may require reasonably, and this on all days excepting when the infirmarer serves out kitchen meats, but even then the cellarer is to serve his rations to the hebdomadary.
alpsn10{_}2792{_}Item, he is to make a pittance of dumplings with seasoning to the convent on the first of the rogation days; each monk and each servant is to have five dumplings uncooked with his seasoning, and one cooked with [oil?]
alpsn10{_}2793{_}and a quart of bread and wine, and each monk is to have one quarter of a pound of cheese.
alpsn10{_}2794{_}Item, upon Holy Thursday he is to give to the convent a pittance of leeks and fish to the value of sixty soldi, and .
alpsn10{_}2795{_}.
alpsn10{_}2796{_}.
alpsn10{_}2797{_}{67} Item, another pittance upon the first day of August; and he is to present the convent with a good sheep and cabbages with seasoning.
alpsn10{_}2798{_}Item, in infirmary time he is to provide two pittances, one of fowls and the other of salt meat and white chestnuts, and he is to give two pints of wine.
alpsn10{_}2799{_}Item, in each week he is to give one flagon [?].
alpsn10{_}2800{_}{68}  Item, the cellarer is to provide napkins and plates at meal times in the refectory, and he is to find the bread for making seasoning, and the vinegar for the mustard; and he is to do an O in Advent, and in Lent he is to provide white chestnuts, and cicerate all the year.
alpsn10{_}2801{_}From the feast of St. Luke to the octave of St. Martin he is to provide fresh chestnuts, to wit, on feasts of twelve lessons; and on dumpling days he is to find the oil and flour with which to make the dumplings.
alpsn10{_}2802{_}"Item, as to the office of surveyor, it is ordered that the surveyor do pay the master builder and also the wages of the day labourers; the lord abbot is to find all the materials requisite for this purpose.
alpsn10{_}2803{_}Item, the surveyor is to make good any plank or post or nail, and he is to repair any hole in the roofs which can be repaired easily, and any beam or piece of boarding.
alpsn10{_}2804{_}Touching the aforesaid materials it is to be understood that the lord abbot furnish beams, boards, rafters, scantling, tiles, and anything of this description; {69} the said surveyor is also to renew the roof of the cloister, chapter, refectory, dormitory, and portico; and the said surveyor is to do an O in Advent.
alpsn10{_}2805{_}"Item, concerning the office of porter.
alpsn10{_}2806{_}The porter is to be in charge of the gate night and day, and if he go outside the convent, he must find a sufficient and trustworthy substitute; on every feast day he is {70} .
alpsn10{_}2807{_}.
alpsn10{_}2808{_}.
alpsn10{_}2809{_}to lose none of his provender; and to receive his clothing in spring as though he were a junior monk; and if he is in holy orders, he is to receive clothing money; and to have his pro rata portions in all distributions.
alpsn10{_}2810{_}Item, the said porter shall enjoy the income derived from S. Michael of Canavesio; and when a monk is received into the monastery, he shall pay to the said porter five good sous; and the said porter shall shut the gates of the convent at sunset, and open them at sunrise."
alpsn10{_}2811{_}The rest of the document is little more than a resume of what has been given, and common form to the effect that nothing in the foregoing is to override any orders made by the Holy Apostolic See which may be preserved in the monastery, and that the rights of the Holy See are to be preserved in all respects intact.
alpsn10{_}2812{_}If doubts arise concerning the interpretation of any clause they are to be settled by the abbot and two of the senior monks.

alpsn10{_}2820{_}{5}  In the index that Butler prepared in view of a possible second edition of Alps and Sanctuaries occurs the following entry under the heading "Waitee":  "All wrong; 'waitee' is 'ohe, ti.'"
alpsn10{_}2821{_}He was subsequently compelled to abandon this eminently plausible etymology, for his friend the Avvocato Negri of Casale-Monferrato told him that the mysterious "waitee" is actually a word in the Ticinese dialect, and, if it were written, would appear as "vuaitee."
alpsn10{_}2822{_}It means "stop" or "look here," and is used to attract attention.
alpsn10{_}2823{_}Butler used to couple this little mistake of his with another that he made in The Authoress of the Odyssey, when he said, "Scheria means Jutland--a piece of land jutting out into the sea."
alpsn10{_}2824{_}Jutland, on the contrary, means the land of the Jutes, and has no more to do with jutting than "waitee" has to do with waiting.--R.

alpsn10{_}2870{_}{32}  Butler always regretted that he did not find out about Medea Colleone's passero solitario in time to introduce it into Alps and Sanctuaries.
alpsn10{_}2871{_}Medea was the daughter of Bartolomeo Colleone, the famous condottiere, whose statue adorns the Campo SS.
alpsn10{_}2872{_}Giovanni e Paolo at Venice.
alpsn10{_}2873{_}Like Catullus's Lesbia, whose immortal passer Butler felt sure was also a passero solitario, she had the misfortune to lose her pet.
alpsn10{_}2874{_}Its little body can still be seen in the Capella Colleone, up in the old town at Bergamo, lying on a little cushion on the top of a little column, and behind it there stands a little weeping willow tree whose leaves, cut out in green paper, droop over the corpse.
alpsn10{_}2875{_}In front of the column is the inscription,--"Passer Medeae Colleonis," and the whole is covered by a glass shade about eight inches high.

alpsn10{_}2880{_}Turin, Civelli & Co.  1870.  p. 116.
alpsn10{_}2881{_}{35}  "Item, ordinaverunt quod fiant mandata seu ellemosinae consuetae quae sint valloris quatuor prebendarum religiosorum omni die ut moris est."
alpsn10{_}2882{_}(Claretta, Storia diplomatica, p.
alpsn10{_}2883{_}325.)
alpsn10{_}2884{_}The mandatum generally refers to "the washing of one another's feet," according to the mandate of Christ during the last supper.
alpsn10{_}2885{_}In the Benedictine order, however, with which we are now concerned, alms, in lieu of the actual washing of feet, are alone intended by the word.
alpsn10{_}2886{_}{36}  The prior-claustralis, as distinguished from the prior-major, was the working head of a monastery, and was supposed never, or hardly ever, to leave the precincts.
alpsn10{_}2887{_}He was the vicar-major of the prior-major.
alpsn10{_}2888{_}The prior-major was vice-abbot when the abbot was absent, but he could not exercise the full functions of an abbot.
alpsn10{_}2889{_}The abbot, prior-major, and prior-claustralis may be compared loosely to the master, vice-master, and senior tutor of a large college.
alpsn10{_}2890{_}{37}  "Item, quod dominus abbas teneatur dare quatuor pitancias seu cenas conventui tempore infirmariae, et quatuor sextaria vini ut consuetum est" (Claretta, Storia diplomatica, p. 326).
alpsn10{_}2891{_}The "infirmariae generales" were stated times during which the monks were to let blood--"Stata nimirum tempora quibus sanguis monachis minuebatur, seu vena secabatur."
alpsn10{_}2892{_}(Ducange.)
alpsn10{_}2893{_}There were five "minutiones generales" in each year--namely, in September, Advent, before Lent, after Easter, and after Pentecost.
alpsn10{_}2894{_}The letting of blood was to last three days; after the third day the patients were to return to matins again, and on the fourth they were to receive absolution.
alpsn10{_}2895{_}Bleeding was strictly forbidden at any other than these stated times, unless for grave illness.
alpsn10{_}2896{_}During the time of blood-letting the monks stayed in the infirmary, and were provided with supper by the abbot.
alpsn10{_}2897{_}During the actual operation the brethren sat all together after orderly fashion in a single room, amid silence and singing of psalms.
alpsn10{_}2898{_}{38}  "Item, quod religiosi non audeant in Sancto Ambrosio videlicet in hospiciis concedere ultra duos pastos videlicet officiariis singulis hebdomadis claustrales non de quindecim diebus nisi forte aliquae personae de eorum parentela transeuntes aut nobiles aut tales de quibus verisimiliter non habetur suspicio eos secum morari faciant, et sic intelligatur de officiariis et de claustralibus" (Claretta, Storia diplomatica, p. 326).
alpsn10{_}2899{_}{39}  The two fingers are the barber's, who lets one finger, or two, or three, intervene between the scissors and the head of the person whose hair he is cutting, according to the length of hair he wishes to remain.
alpsn10{_}2900{_}{40}  "Cellelarius teneatur ministrare panem et vinum et pittanciarius pittanciam" (Claretta, Stor.
alpsn10{_}2901{_}dip., p. 327).
alpsn10{_}2902{_}Pittancia is believed to be a corruption of "pietantia."
alpsn10{_}2903{_}"Pietantiae modus et ordo sic conscripti .
alpsn10{_}2904{_}.
alpsn10{_}2905{_}.
alpsn10{_}2906{_}observentur.
alpsn10{_}2907{_}In primis videlicet, quod pietantiarius qui pro tempore fuerit omni anno singulis festivitatibus infra scriptis duo ova in brodio pipere et croco bene condito omnibus et singulis fratribus .
alpsn10{_}2908{_}.
alpsn10{_}2909{_}.
alpsn10{_}2910{_}tenebitur ministrare."
alpsn10{_}2911{_}(Decretum pro Monasterio Dobirluc., A.D. 1374, apud Ducange.)
alpsn10{_}2912{_}A "pittance" ordinarily was served to two persons in a single dish, but there need not be a dish necessarily, for a piece of raw cheese or four eggs would be a pittance.
alpsn10{_}2913{_}The pittancer was the official whose business it was to serve out their pittances to each of the monks.
alpsn10{_}2914{_}Practically he was the maitre d'hotel of the establishment.
alpsn10{_}2915{_}{41}  Here the text seems to be corrupt.
alpsn10{_}2916{_}{42}  That is to say, he is to serve out rations of bread and wine to everyone.
alpsn10{_}2917{_}{43}  "Tres denarios."
alpsn10{_}2918{_}{44}  "Unam carbonatam porci."
alpsn10{_}2919{_}I suppose I have translated this correctly; I cannot find that there is any substance known as "carbonate of pork."
alpsn10{_}2920{_}{45}  "Rapiolla" I presume to be a translation of "raviolo," or "raviuolo," which, as served at San Pietro at the present day, is a small dumpling containing minced meat and herbs, and either boiled or baked according to preference.

alpsn10{_}2947{_}{55}  "Biroleti."
alpsn10{_}2948{_}I have not been able to find the words "carmingier," "primmentum," and "biroletus" in any dictionary.
alpsn10{_}2949{_}"Biroletus" is probably the same as "luiroletus" which we have met with above, and the word is misprinted in one or both cases.
alpsn10{_}2950{_}{56}  "Item, priori claustrali pro sua dupla sex florinos."
alpsn10{_}2951{_}"Dupla" has the meaning "mulcta" assigned to it in Ducange among others, none of which seem appropriate here.
alpsn10{_}2952{_}The translation as above, however, is not satisfactory.
alpsn10{_}2953{_}{57}  "Pastamderio."
alpsn10{_}2954{_}I have been unable to find this word in any dictionary.
alpsn10{_}2955{_}The text in this part is evidently full of misprints and corruptions.
alpsn10{_}2956{_}{58}  "Ciceratam fractam."
alpsn10{_}2957{_}This word is not given in any dictionary.
alpsn10{_}2958{_}Cicer is a small kind of pea, so cicerata fracta may perhaps mean something like pease pudding.
alpsn10{_}2959{_}{59}  Terce.
alpsn10{_}2960{_}A service of the Roman Church.
alpsn10{_}2961{_}{60}  "Invitatorium."
alpsn10{_}2962{_}Ce nom est donne a un verset qui se chante ou se recite au commencement de l'office de marines.
alpsn10{_}2963{_}Il varie selon les fetes et meme les feries.
alpsn10{_}2964{_}Migne.
alpsn10{_}2965{_}Encyclopedie Theologique.
alpsn10{_}2966{_}{61}  "Epistolam Evangelii."
alpsn10{_}2967{_}There are probably several misprints here.
alpsn10{_}2968{_}{62}  "Monnas."
alpsn10{_}2969{_}Word not to be found.
alpsn10{_}2970{_}{63}  "Sextaria."
alpsn10{_}2971{_}{64}  Word missing in the original.
alpsn10{_}2972{_}{65}  "Borchiam."
alpsn10{_}2973{_}Word not to be found.
alpsn10{_}2974{_}Borchia in Italian is a kind of ornamental boss.
alpsn10{_}2975{_}{66}  "Teneatur dare religiosis de carnibus bovinis et montonis decenter."
alpsn10{_}2976{_}{67}  "Foannotos."
alpsn10{_}2977{_}Word not to be found.

alpsn10{_}2980{_}{70}  "In processionibus deferre et de sua prebenda nihil perdat vestiarium vere suum salvatur eidem sicut uni monacullo."
